Title: MixCIT: A Kernel Based Local-Polynomial Debiased Test for Conditional Independence on Mixed-Type Data

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2607.12830v1

Published Time: Wed, 15 Jul 2026 11:58:10 GMT

Number of Pages: 101

Markdown Content:
# MixCIT: A Kernel Based Local-Polynomial Debiased Test for Conditional Independence on Mixed-Type Data 

## Mengxiao Gao ∗1, Kyra Gan †1, and Promit Ghosal ‡21School of Operation Research and Information Engineering, Cornell University 

2Department of Statistics, University of Chicago July 15, 2026 

Abstract 

Conditional independence testing (CIT) is fundamental to modern statistical inference in areas related to causal discovery and variable selection. While marginal independence is relatively well-understood, despite multiple advances, no existing non-parametric CIT pro-vides a unified, efficient, and statistically guaranteed solution across heterogeneous data. We introduce a graph-based test statistic comparing kernel similarities of the response within composite neighborhoods that use exact matching on discrete components and kn-nearest-neighbor matching on continuous ones. The raw statistic, related to prior constructions [Huang et al., 2022], suffices under fully discrete conditioning. However, when at least one conditioning variable is continuous, we instead use a local-polynomial debiased variant that cancels the local smoothing bias. We rigorously establish its asymptotic null distribu-tion across all data-type combinations. We further prove a dimension-free n−1/4 detection threshold under local alternatives, eliminating the phase transition that affects geometric estimators in high dimensions. Finally, we develop efficient algorithms with near-quadratic complexity and analytic graph-based calibration, bypassing the cubic bottlenecks of global kernel methods. 

Keywords: Conditional independence, Graph-based estimator, Asymptotic distribution, Power analysis 

# 1 Introduction 

Let ( X, Y, Z) be random variables taking values in X , Y, Z, with joint distribution P . Condi-tional independence testing (CIT) which decides whether X ⊥⊥ Y | Z based on an i.i.d. sample 

{(Xi, Y i, Zi)}ni=1 is fundamental to modern statistical inference. It underpins constraint-based causal discovery algorithms [Spirtes et al., 2000, Pearl, 2000, Chickering, 2002, Tsamardinos et al., 2006, Colombo and Maathuis, 2014], which execute a polynomial (or exponential in worst case) number of CITs to infer directed acyclic graphs; additive noise model-based causal infer-ence [Hoyer et al., 2009, Peters et al., 2016, Zhang and Hyv¨ arinen, 2009], where CIT validates the absence of confounding; local causal discovery methods [Pe˜ na et al., 2007, Aliferis et al., 2010] that scale to high-dimensional settings by restricting search neighborhoods; variable selection [George, 2000, Fan and Li, 2001, Barut et al., 2016], where predictors are retained only if they remain relevant after conditioning on others; and sufficient dimension reduction [Cook and Li, 2002, Li and Wang, 2007, Fukumizu et al., 2009], where the goal is to find the lowest-dimensional subspace of Z capturing all information about Y . In all these applications, reliable CIT directly determines the quality of downstream inference.

mg2532@cornell.edu

1arXiv:2607.12830v1 [math.ST] 14 Jul 2026 1.1 Background and Existing Approaches 

While marginal independence ( Z = ∅) is relatively well understood with theoretically grounded nonparametric solutions for continuous variables (distance correlation [Sz´ ekely et al., 2007, Sz´ ekely and Rizzo, 2009], Hilbert-Schmidt Independence Criterion (HSIC) [Gretton et al., 2005, 2008], randomized dependence coefficient [Lopez-Paz et al., 2013]), discrete variables (Pearson’s χ2 test [Pearson, 1900], exact tests [Agresti, 2012]), and recent graph-based and kernel/distance-based independence tests that can be adapted to mixed data [Heller et al., 2013, Sejdinovic et al., 2013, Deb et al., 2020], the conditional variant introduces substantial statistical and computa-tional complexities. Adjusting for a potentially high-dimensional, heterogeneous conditioning set 

Z necessitates flexible nonparametric frameworks that avoid restrictive parametric assumptions while simultaneously requiring computational scalability for applications such as causal discovery, where tests may be executed multiple times. Existing methodological development has progressed along data-type-specific regimes which we describe in what follows. 

Discrete data. For purely discrete ( X, Y, Z), exact stratification approaches provide princi-pled solutions. The Cochran–Mantel–Haenszel test [Cochran, 1954, Mantel and Haenszel, 1959] and log-linear models [Agresti, 2012, Bishop et al., 2007] leverage combinatorial structure to as-sess conditional independence without smoothing. However, these methods suffer from the curse of dimensionality: with |Z| = d discrete variables each taking L values, sample sizes must scale exponentially ( n ≫ Ld) to ensure adequate cell counts, rendering them impractical when d is large. 

Continuous data. In purely continuous settings, diverse nonparametric approaches have emerged. kernel methods adopt a global RKHS perspective, with the Kernel Conditional Inde-pendence (KCI) test [Zhang et al., 2011] and its approximations [Strobl et al., 2019] estimating conditional mean embeddings via kernel ridge regression to test whether the conditional cross-covariance operator Σ XY |Z vanishes, while the Generalized Covariance Measure (GCM) [Shah and Peters, 2020] uses similar ideas for instrumental variables; these methods assume smooth variation across the support of Z and incur O(n3) cost, with rigorous RKHS foundations [Fuku-mizu et al., 2009, 2008, Park and Muandet, 2021] but persistent computational bottlenecks. 

Information-theoretic methods use nearest-neighbor (NN) estimators for conditional mutual in-formation I(X; Y |Z) [Runge, 2018, Frenzel and Pompe, 2007, Vejmelka and Palus, 2008, Gao et al., 2015, Runge et al., 2019, Niles-Weed and Berthet, 2021], achieving O(n log n) efficiency but requiring unified metrics on ( Z, X ) that become ambiguous for mixed data. Conditional distance covariance extends distance correlation [Wang et al., 2015, Huang, 2010b], with the Pro-jected Covariance Measure (PCM) [Lundborg et al., 2024] projecting onto the orthogonal com-plement of Z-variation, achieving √n-consistency but requiring careful bandwidth selection and struggling with high-dimensional Z. Rank-based methods build on Chatterjee [2021]’s marginal coefficient with finite-sample guarantees [Shi et al., 2022, Deb et al., 2020], with recent extensions approximating conditional dependence via NN-defined Z-neighborhoods [Azadkia and Chatter-jee, 2021, Azadkia et al., 2022] and theoretical extensions to multivariate Y establishing CLTs under independence [Huang et al., 2026]. 

Mixed discrete-continuous data. When ( X, Y, Z) comprises mixed components, existing approaches face fundamental obstacles. kernel methods require product kernels on mixed spaces, which remains theoretically delicate [Szab´ o and Sriperumbudur, 2018]. Discretization approaches 

force continuous components into bins [Huang, 2010a, Zhang, 2008], inducing bias, information loss, and curse of dimensionality as bin numbers grow exponentially with dimension. Model-based methods such as mixed graphical models [Yang et al., 2015, Lee and Hastie, 2015, Chen et al., 2015, Fellinghauer et al., 2013] assume parametric conditional distributions (e.g., conditional Gaussians for continuous nodes, conditional exponential families for discrete nodes), limiting robustness to model misspecification. Recent work on nonparametric mixed-data independence testing spans three lines: kernel-based tests with product kernels extending the Hilbert-Schmidt Independence Criterion of Gret-ton et al. [2005]; copula-based procedures [Genest and Favre, 2007] requiring semiparametric 2assumptions on the marginals; and permutation-based classifier tests [Sen et al., 2017, Kim et al., 2021] that lack explicit asymptotic theory. Each targets marginal rather than conditional indepen-dence. Critically, no existing nonparametric CIT framework provides (i) geometrically principled neighborhoods for mixed ( X, Y, Z), (ii) complete asymptotic null distributions across all data-type regimes, (iii) power analysis characterizing detection thresholds, and (iv) near-quadratic computational complexity with direct p-value calibration. 

## 1.2 Our Approach and Contributions 

We address this gap through a unified geometric framework that reframes CIT as a comparison of local alignments . Under H0 : Y ⊥⊥ X | Z, the conditional distribution of Y given Z re-mains unchanged when further conditioned on X. Consequently, the local variation of Y within neighborhoods defined by Z alone should statistically align with its variation within neighbor-hoods defined jointly by ( Z, X ). A systematic discrepancy signals conditional dependence. This intuition naturally accommodates mixed data if neighborhoods are constructed appropriately. We incorporate this idea using the notion of composite neighborhoods . For discrete com-ponents, we enforce exact matching; for continuous components, we use kn-nearest neighbors in metric spaces. This yields neighborhoods that are geometrically faithful without artificial embeddings or arbitrary binning, and adapts automatically to local data support. From these neighborhoods, we form a raw difference ∆ n comparing average kernel similarities of Y within the two neighborhood types. When both Z and X are discrete, exact matching introduces no smoothing bias and ∆ n itself is a degenerate U -statistic with a mixture-of-chi-squared limit. When at least one of Z, X has a continuous component, however, ∆ n acquires a non-vanishing smoothing bias of order ( kn/n )2/D which divides √n and obstructs pivotal inference. In these continuous-or-mixed regimes we work with a local-polynomial debiased statistic ∆ (p) 

> n

that replaces the inner kn-NN averages by intercepts of a degree-p weighted least-squares fit of K(Yi, ·) on the neighborhood; the local-polynomial reproduction identities cancel the smoothing bias up to order 

p + 1, restoring the √n-pivotal property. 

Contribution 1: Regime-adapted test statistics. We propose a two-statistic construc-tion tied to the data type of ( Z, X ). For all-discrete conditioning, we use the raw ∆ n from Equa-tion (1), which suffices because exact matching is bias-free. For all other regimes, we use the debi-ased ∆ (p) 

> n

in Equation (4), built from local-polynomial intercepts over composite coarse neighbor-hoods Ci (based on Z) and fine neighborhoods Fi (based on ( Z, X )). For mixed Z = ( Z(d), Z(c)), neighborhoods use a composite metric giving primary priority to exact matching in discrete di-mensions and secondary refinement via Euclidean distance in continuous dimensions. Crucially, the local-polynomial weights w(p)

The construction recovers the graph-based Kernel Partial Cor-relation estimator [Huang et al., 2022] when ( Z, X ) has discrete support, but differs significantly when ( Z, X ) has continuous components.

The procedure scales gracefully to n ∼ 10 4–10 5 and is viable for causal discovery pipelines executing thousands of CITs. Notably, our framework includes marginal independence ( Z = ∅) as a special case, providing a consistent nonparametric test for mixed ( X, Y ) without requiring separate marginal procedures. 

## 1.3 Organization 

The remainder of the paper is organized as follows. Section 2 formalizes the problem, describes composite neighborhood construction, defines the test statistic, establishes its U -statistic rep-resentation, and connects it to maximum mean discrepancy. Section 3 presents the complete asymptotic theory under H0 for all four regimes, along with consistency and power analysis. Section 4 provides computational algorithms for neighborhood construction and regime-specific calibration procedures. Section 5 reports extensive simulation studies comparing our test to exist-ing methods across diverse data types, dimensions, and sample sizes, plus applications to genomic data with mixed genotype-expression-covariate structures. Section 6 concludes with discussion of limitations and future directions. All proofs and technical lemmas appear in the Appendix. 

# 2 A Unified Test Statistic for Data of Potentially Mixed Types 

Let ( X, Y, Z) be random variables with joint distribution P , taking values in domains X , Y, Z. In our setting, X and Y could be either discrete or continuous variables with dimension 1, whereas 

Z could be a multi-dimensional variable, with each component of Z being either discrete or continuous—allowing Z to be of mixed data types. Given n i.i.d. samples {(Xi, Y i, Zi)}ni=1 , we test the null hypothesis of conditional independence: 

H0 : Y ⊥⊥ X | Z versus H1 : Y̸ ⊥⊥ X | Z.

4Our approach relies on a simple geometric intuition: under H0, knowing X provides no additional information about Y once Z is known. We operationalize this by comparing the average similarity of Y values in two types of local neighborhoods: (1) Coarse Neighborhoods (Ci): Points similar to i based on Z alone, (2) Fine Neighborhoods (Fi): Points similar to i based on both ( Z, X ). If H0 holds, the average similarity of Y values within the fine neighborhood should match that in the coarse neighborhood. If X provides additional information about Y , then Y values will be more similar within fine neighborhoods than within coarse neighborhoods. 

## 2.1 Neighborhood Construction for Mixed Data 

To accommodate mixed data types, we construct neighborhoods using a stratified nearest-neighbor approach that treats discrete and continuous components differently. For a discrete variable U

(e.g., a component of Z or X), we define an exact-match constraint :

d(Ui, U j ) = 

> {

0 if Ui = Uj

∞ otherwise This enforces hard stratification: observations with mismatched discrete values are excluded from neighborhood consideration. For a continuous variable U , we use the Euclidean distance: 

d(Ui, U j ) = ∥Ui − Uj ∥2. When constructing neighborhoods in mixed-type settings, we apply a 

stratified k-NN procedure: 1. First, restrict the search to the subset of samples that match exactly on all discrete com-ponents; 2. Within that subset, select the k nearest neighbors based on Euclidean distance in the continuous components. This procedure implements a dissimilarity measure on ( Z, X ) where discrete components act as hard constraints. Let Ci denote the indices of neighbors of observation i based on Z alone (coarse neighborhood), and let Fi denote the indices of neighbors based on ( Z, X ) (fine neighborhood). We assume k < n is either fixed or grows slowly with n (e.g., k ≍ nγ for some γ ∈ (0 , 1)). Crucially, when fewer than k points satisfy the exact-match constraint on discrete components, the neighborhood contains all available points in that stratum. Let ci = |C i| and fi = |F i| denote the actual neighborhood sizes, which satisfy 1 ≤ fi ≤ ci ≤ k.

## 2.2 Test Statistics

[2010, 2011]) such as the Gaussian K(y, y ′) = exp( −∥ y − y′∥2/σ 2) (bandwidth σ from the median heuristic) for continuous Y . The two inner averages estimate E[K(Yi, Y )|Zi, X i] and E[K(Yi, Y )|Zi] respectively. Their difference quantifies whether incorporating X changes the local structure of Y beyond what Z alone determines. # 3 Theoretical Asymptotic Properties 

In this section, we establish the asymptotic behavior of the debiased test statistic ∆ n (when ( Z, X )have discrete support) or, ∆ (p) 

> n

(when ( Z, X ) has continuous components) under H0 : Y ⊥⊥ X | Z.A unifying finding is that, across all four data-type regimes for ( Z, X ), the debiased statistic admits a Gaussian limit at the √n-scale, with the smoothing bias of the raw statistic killed by the local-polynomial reproduction identities. The variance constant τ 2 

> p

which varies by regime, is driven by dimensional mismatch in the all-continuous case and by radius mismatch in the mixed cases, but remains strictly positive under mild non-degeneracy. We present the main results here; complete proofs and regularity conditions appear in the Appendix. 

## 3.1 Discrete Neighborhoods: Mixture of Chi-Squared Distribution 

When both Z and X are discrete, neighborhoods are constructed via exact matching: Ci = {j :

Zj = Zi} and Fi = {j : ( Zj , X j ) = ( Zi, X i)}. Under mild regularity ensuring that empiri-cal neighborhood sizes grow to infinity (see Assumptions in Theorem 7 of Section D.1 in the Appendix), the test statistic exhibits a degenerate U -statistic structure. 

Theorem 1 (Null Distribution: Discrete Case) . Suppose Z and X are discrete with finite support, and Assumption S1 holds. Under H0 : Y ⊥⊥ X | Z, as n → ∞ :

S defined in the Appendix. The proof is given in Appendix D.4. 

Remark 1 (Comparison with Existing Tests) . The mixture-of-chi-squared limit (7) is charac-teristic of degenerate second-order U -statistics with compact operator kernels. When applied to a discrete conditioning variable, the Kernel Conditional Independence test (KCI) of Zhang et al. [2011] admits the same weighted-chi-squared limit under H0. On the oher hand, two related meth-ods differ in the following way: the Generalized Covariance Measure of Shah and Peters [2020] is calibrated by a Gaussian limit rather than a quadratic form, and the Conditional Random-ization Test of Cand` es et al. [2018] draws its finite-sample validity from the known conditional law of X | Z rather than any asymptotic distributional guarantee. Classical dense KCI further require an O(n3) matrix operation; our Gaussian multiplier bootstrap matches its asymptotic va-lidity in O(n2) time (Section 4.1) by exploiting the sparse block structure of S induced by discrete neighborhoods. 

## 3.2 Null Gaussian Limit for the Debiased Statistic 

The local-polynomial debiasing in (3)–(4) eliminates the smoothing bias that obstructs √n-pivotal inference for the raw statistic ∆ n in the presence of continuous components. The resulting debiased statistic ∆ (p) 

> n

admits a Gaussian limit uniformly across all four data-type regimes for (Z, X ).

Remark 2 (Comparison with continuous-conditioning tests) . The Gaussian limit (8) unifies all three regimes of (Z, X ) at the √n-scale, contrasting with the mixture-of-chi-squared limit that arises for the raw statistic ∆n under purely discrete conditioning. The √n-normality is broadly consistent with recent kernel-based and projection-based conditional independence tests, including the Conditional Correlation Test of Wang et al. [2015], the Projected Covariance Measure of Lundborg et al. [2024], and the Conditional Randomization Test of Cand` es et al. [2018], although these rely on different statistics (rank correlations, projected kernels, model-X formulations).

Theorem 3 (Universal Consistency of the Debiased Test) . Suppose the kernel K is bounded and characteristic, and the local-polynomial weights satisfy the design regularity of Lemma 9 with 

kn → ∞ and kn/n → 0 (no undersmoothing required). Under any alternative H1 : Y̸ ⊥⊥ X | Z,

Remark 3 (Universal consistency in the literature) . Universal consistency under characteristic kernels is a hallmark of nonparametric tests based on conditional mean embeddings. It is shared by KCI [Zhang et al., 2011], the Hilbert-Schmidt Conditional Independence Criterion [Fukumizu et al., 2008], the Randomized Conditional Independence Test [Strobl et al., 2019], and the kernel partial correlation of Huang et al. [2022]. Our result extends this guarantee to the debiased neighborhood-based statistic, which has the computational advantage of avoiding full kernel-matrix operations and the inferential advantage of an asymptotically standard normal pivot ( (8) ). The population measure T (P ) is closely related with the conditional Maximum Mean Discrepancy of Huang et al. [2022], providing a population-level interpretation of the debiased statistic. The consistency rate under fixed alternatives is the parametric - n in the all discrete case and √n

when at least one component is continuous. 

## 3.4 Local Power Analysis: Detection Threshold 

To characterize the test’s ability to detect weak departures from H0, we consider local alternatives converging to the null at rate rn → 0. We focus on the continuous/mixed case (Theorem 2). Consider the mixture model (X, Y, Z) ∼ frn := (1 − rn) fZ fX|Z fY |Z + rn gX,Y,Z , (11) 8where the null component fZ fX|Z fY |Z enforces conditional independence, gX,Y,Z is an alternative density preserving the Z and X|Z marginals, and rn → 0 controls proximity to H0. The one-sided level-α test is Sn := 1{T (p) 

> n

≥ zα} with T (p) 

> n

= √n ∆(p) 

> n

/̂τp and ̂ τ 2 

> p

a consistent estimator of τ 2

> p

obtained via the overlap-graph plug-in estimator (Section 4.2). 

Theorem 4 (Dimension-free detection threshold) . Suppose the conditions of Theorem 2 hold under the local alternative (11) , with regularity conditions on fZ , f X|Z , f Y |Z,X , g X,Y,Z (detailed in Assumption 3 of the Appendix). Let ϕ(rn) := Pfrn {Sn = 1 } denote the power. Then for any dimensions dZ , d X ≥ 0,

• If rn ≪ n−1/4, then ϕ(rn) → α;

• If rn ≫ n−1/4, then ϕ(rn) → 1.The detection threshold for the mixture parameter is therefore r∗ 

> n

≍ n−1/4, dimension-free. 

The proof, which combines the null CLT (Theorem 2) with the signal expansion T (Pfrn ) = 

r2

> n

ηalt + O(r3

> n

) (with ηalt > 0 the population signal strength), is given in Appendix J. The key simplification afforded by the debiased construction is that the mean expansion of √n ∆(p) 

> n

under the mixture has only one √n-scale term: Efrn [√n ∆(p)

] = √n r 2

> n

ηalt + o(1). The two dimension-dependent geometric biases at rates n1/2−2/(dZ +dX ) and n1/2−2/d Z that appear in the analysis of the raw statistic are exactly cancelled by the local-polynomial reproduction identities coming from (3). 

Remark 4 (Comparison with power analysis in the literature) . Local power analysis for con-ditional independence tests remains relatively sparse. Notable exceptions include Bergsma and Dassios [2014], who analyze partial-correlation tests under Gaussian alternatives, and Shah and Peters [2020], who prove a no-free-lunch hardness result for nonparametric CIT whereas minimax theory for discrete or binned conditional independence is studied in Neykov et al. [2021]. Our re-sult establishes the parametric n−1/4 detection threshold uniformly across dimensions, recovering the n−1/4 rate that Bhattacharya [2019] obtain for two sample testing in the lower-dimensional regime d ≤ 8. Where the other geometric graph based statistic exhibits a phase transition at 

dZ + dX = 8 , the debiased statistic eliminates this transition by killing the dimension-dependent geometric biases, restoring the parametric rate at all dimensions. This is the central inferential benefit of debiasing. 

Remark 5 (Practical implications) . For applications in causal discovery where conditional in-dependence tests are applied repeatedly with varying conditioning sets, Theorem 4 confirms that the debiased statistic achieves uniform n−1/4 detection across regimes, with no need to switch between test variants by dimension. The Appendix provides guidance on selecting the polynomial order p and bandwidth kn to balance bias-cancellation against design-matrix conditioning; the recommendation p = ⌈D/ 2⌉ minimizes Np while satisfying the undersmoothing constraint. 

## 3.5 Summary of Asymptotic Results 

Table 1 summarizes the asymptotic properties across different data regimes. The theoretical developments in this section provide a rigorous foundation for the compu-tational procedures presented in Section 4, enabling both valid hypothesis testing and power-optimal inference across a wide range of data types. Complete proofs, technical assumptions, and extensions (including higher-order asymptotics and finite-sample corrections) are provided in the Appendix.

The asymptotic distribution theory established in Section 3 enables efficient calibration of hy-pothesis tests without requiring extensive resampling procedures. Unlike kernel-based conditional independence tests such as KCI [Zhang et al., 2011], which require O(n3) matrix operation of dense kernel matrices, our approach lever-ages sparsity in the neighborhood structure to achieve substantially reduced computational com-plexity. 1. Discrete neighborhoods (Theorem 1): When Z and X are purely discrete, the test statistic n∆n converges in distribution to an infinite weighted sum of chi-squared random variables, ∑∞

We instead employ the Gaussian multiplier bootstrap to simulate from the lim-iting distribution without explicit eigendecomposition, achieving O(n) or O(n2) complexity depending on whether Y is discrete or continuous. 2. Continuous or mixed neighborhoods (Theorem 2): When at least one of Z or X is con-tinuous, the test statistic √n∆n is asymptotically N (0 , σ 2) where σ2 = 4Var( E[h(W1, W 2) |

W1]). We estimate the scalar variance σ2 and empirical mean of proposed test statistic using a small number of conditional permutations (resampling Y within Z-defined neigh-borhoods), then calibrate the test analytically using the standard normal distribution. The overall complexity is O(P n 2) for P permutations, with P ≪ 1000 typically sufficient in practice. Both strategies represent substantial computational gains over standard permutation-based nonparametric tests, which typically require ∼ 10 3 permutations to accurately estimate null distributions.

4.1.2 Exploiting Sparsity via Neighborhood Structure 

The operator S admits a sparse representation arising from the discrete neighborhood structure. Let PZ ∈ { 0, 1}mZ ×n and PZX ∈ { 0, 1}mZX ×n be indicator matrices encoding group memberships, where mZ and mZX are the numbers of distinct values of Z and ( Z, X ) respectively. For discrete data, mZ , m ZX ≪ n typically. Let wZ ∈ RmZ and wZX ∈ RmZX denote weight vectors inversely proportional to group sizes. The operator can be expressed as:

4.2.2 Neighborhood Construction and Distance Metrics 

Accurate neighborhood identification is critical. We employ the following strategies. 

Standardization. All continuous components of Z and X are standardized to zero mean and unit variance before distance computation, to prevent scale-dependent domination by high-variance variables. 

Composite metric for mixed data. When Z or ( Z, X ) contains both discrete and continuous components, denote Z = ( Zdisc , Zcont ). We define a hierarchical distance 

d((zi, x i), (zℓ, x ℓ)) =

> {

ε d Euc (zi, cont , z ℓ, cont ) if discrete components match, 

∞ otherwise, (25) where dEuc is the Euclidean distance on standardized continuous variables and ε > 0 is a small constant ensuring within-stratum ranking by continuous proximity. Exact matching on discrete components is prioritized; continuous variables serve as tie-breakers. 

Neighborhood size selection. The undersmoothing constraint √n (kn/n )(p+1) /D → 0 from Theorem 2 requires kn = nα with α < 1 − D/ (2( p + 1)). With p = ⌈D/ 2⌉ (the minimum-cost choice from Section 3), this admits α up to nearly 1 /2, so a default kn ≈ c√n with c ∈ [1 , 5] satisfies the condition; for higher polynomial orders, larger kn is permitted. Data-driven selection of kn via cross-validation is possible but not pursued here. 

4.2.3 Algorithmic Details 

Algorithm 2 in Appendix K.2 gives the complete procedure. Key points: 1. Efficient kn-NN construction. Using spatial data structures (KD-trees or ball trees), the fine and coarse kn-NN graphs are built in O(n log n) time on average for moderate dimensions. 2. Local-polynomial fits. For each anchor i, the intercepts ̂ a(p) 

> i,F

,̂ a(p) 

> i,C

are obtained by solving the Np × Np normal equations for the weighted least-squares problem (3); this is O(knN 2 

> p

+

N 3 

> p

) per anchor. 13 3. Overlap variance. The double sum (23) restricts to pairs ( i, ℓ ) with ωiℓ = 1. Since each anchor has O(kn) neighbors in either graph, the effective number of nonzero terms is 

O(nk n), computed in O(nk n) time after the NN graphs are constructed. 4. Analytic calibration. The p-value is computed analytically from (24); no resampling is required.

# 5 Numerical Studies 

To comprehensively evaluate the robustness of our method, we generate synthetic datasets en-compassing continuous, discrete, and mixed data types under both linear and nonlinear causal mechanisms. Detailed descriptions of the data-generating processes and parameter configurations are provided in Appendix K.3. 

## 5.1 Scalability and Performance of the Proposed Method 

We first evaluate the scalability and statistical performance of our proposed debiased algorithm across varying sample sizes, ranging from N = 1000 to N = 10000. As shown in Table 2, our method consistently controls the Type-I error around the nominal level across purely continuous (CCC - X, Y, Z all are continuous), purely discrete (DDD - X, Y, Z all are discrete), and mixed (DCC - X discrete and Y, Z continuous, CCD - X, Y continuous and Z discrete) regimes for both linear and nonlinear mechanisms. While statistical power naturally starts slightly lower in challenging nonlinear regimes at smaller sample sizes (e.g., 0.75 for CCC nonlinear at N = 1000), it rapidly converges to approximately 1.00 as the sample size increases. Most notably, our algorithm exhibits exceptional computational scalability. For N = 10000, the average runtime remains strictly under 10 seconds across all tested regimes. This unprece-dented efficiency demonstrates that our approach is highly practical for large-scale datasets where traditional non-parametric tests would become computationally intractable. 

## 5.2 Comparison Against Baseline Algorithms 

Building upon the robust performance of our proposed method, we further benchmark it against three widely used baseline algorithms: KCI, Fisher-z, and CMI-KNN. This comparison, sum-marized in Table 3, spans sample sizes from N = 1000 to N = 5000 to highlight the trade-offs inherent in existing approaches. 14 Table 2: Scalability and performance of the proposed method (Ours) across sample sizes N ∈{1000 , 2000 , 5000 , 10000 }. Validity and Type-I Error Control A critical limitation of current methods is the inability to maintain valid Type-I error control across diverse data regimes. Fisher-z, being strictly linear, understandably fails in nonlinear scenarios, exhibiting severe Type-I error inflation (e.g., reaching 0.55 in the CCC nonlinear regime at N = 5000). While KCI and CMI-KNN offer non-parametric flexibility, KCI shows unexpected error inflation in specific mixed-data nonlinear cases as the sample size grows (e.g., 0 .15 in DCC nonlinear at N = 5000), indicating potential instability in kernel hyperparameter selection. In contrast, as established in the previous section, our debiased algorithm maintains strict validity across all tested scenarios. 

Statistical Power Limitations Regarding statistical power, KCI serves as a strong upper-bound baseline, reliably detecting dependencies across all regimes. However, the alternative non-parametric method, CMI-KNN, struggles significantly in mixed-data settings. For instance, its power stagnates around 0 .58 ∼ 0.59 in the DCC linear regime regardless of the sample size. Our proposed algorithm effectively overcomes the detection failures seen in CMI-KNN, matching the high statistical power of KCI as N increases. 

The Computational Bottleneck The most stark contrast between our method and the base-lines lies in computational scalability. While KCI provides excellent statistical power, its runtime exhibits a prohibitive non-linear growth with respect to the sample size. As N increases from 1000 to 5000, KCI’s average runtime skyrockets from approximately 1 second to nearly 2 minutes (over 100 seconds) per test. CMI-KNN, though faster than KCI in high-sample regimes, still consistently requires more computation time than our approach in mixed datasets. This compar-ison underscores the primary contribution of our work: achieving the rigorous statistical power of heavy kernel methods without inheriting their computational bottlenecks, seamlessly analyzing even 10000 samples in mere seconds. 

## 5.3 Robustness to Non-Ordinal Heterogeneous Confounding 

To systematically evaluate the robustness of conditional independence tests in mixed-data regimes, we investigate the sensitivity of the tests to non-ordinal heterogeneous confounding. We introduce a shift parameter δ ≥ 0 that controls the amplitude of a “sawtooth” effect induced by a discrete confounder. The results for both linear and nonlinear continuous confounding mechanisms at sample size N = 1000 are summarized in Table 4. As shown in Table 4, when δ = 0, the underlying distribution reduces to a homogeneous regime. Here, both KCI and our proposed MixCIT reasonably control the Type-I error around the nominal level (with MixCIT being slightly more conservative). However, as the heterogeneity strength δ increases, the performance of KCI deteriorates drastically. The proposed method scales efficiently while maintaining high statistical power and valid Type-I errors across all regimes.

Chi-sq 0.00 / 1.00 / 0.00s 0.00 / 1.00 / 0.00s 0.00 / 1.00 / 0.00s G-sq 0.00 / 1.00 / 0.00s 0.00 / 1.00 / 0.00s 0.00 / 1.00 / 0.00s 16 categories into a continuous metric space, KCI erroneously applies smooth radial basis function (RBF) kernels across distinct, non-ordinal categories. This cross-stratum kernel leakage induces a severe residual confounding bias. Consequently, the Type-I error of KCI escalates monotonically, reaching up to 1 .00 at δ = 0 .30, completely invalidating the test. In stark contrast, our MixCIT algorithm explicitly enforces exact stratification on the discrete components. This structural isolation guarantees that samples from different discrete categories never interact during the local-polynomial fitting phase, neutralizing the cross-stratum leakage. As a result, MixCIT is mathematically immune to the heterogeneity magnitude δ. It flawlessly controls the Type-I error at 0 .00 across the entire spectrum of δ while maintaining exceptionally high statistical power ( ≥ 0.96). Furthermore, by avoiding the computation of a global dense kernel matrix, MixCIT demonstrates significant computational advantages, operating approximately 7 ×

faster than KCI on average ( ∼0.08 seconds vs. ∼0.57 seconds per trial). Table 4: Empirical Type-I error and Power across varying heterogeneity strengths ( δ) for N =1000 at significance level α = 0 .05. MixCIT maintains strict Type-I error control regardless of δ,whereas KCI suffers from severe false positives due to cross-stratum leakage.

# 6 Discussion 

Despite its strong empirical performance, MixCIT has two inherent limitations that suggest fruitful directions for future work. First, the exact-matching stratification on discrete covariates can lead to severe sparsity when 

Z contains high-cardinality or multiple categorical variables. The number of possible strata grows exponentially with the number of discrete components, causing many strata to contain too few observations for stable local-polynomial estimation. A promising remedy is fuzzy stratification ,where observations from similar discrete states contribute with a similarity weight, pooling infor-mation across strata via learned embeddings. Second, the median-heuristic bandwidth selection for the kernel on Y is not optimal for detect-ing weak or localized nonlinear signals. A global bandwidth may either oversmooth the signal or inflate variance. Future extensions could adopt adaptive bandwidth selection or a multiple-kernel mixture to enhance power across diverse dependence structures. Addressing these issues would broaden MixCIT’s applicability to high-dimensional discrete settings and improve its robustness to complex dependencies.

In this section, we review standard results on U -statistics that are essential for our proofs. Let 

W1, . . . , W n be i.i.d. random variables taking values in a measurable space W with distribution 

P . We first give the definition of a U -statistic and Hilbert-Schmidt operator. 

Definition 1 (U -statistic and Degeneracy) . A U -statistic of order 2 with a symmetric kernel 

h : W2 → R is defined as: 

Un =

(n

2

)−1 ∑

> 1≤i<j ≤n

h(Wi, W j ).

Let θ = E[h(W1, W 2)] . The first-order projection is defined as ψ1(w) = E[h(w, W 2)] − θ. The 

U -statistic is called degenerate of order 1 if Var (ψ1(W1)) = 0 .

Definition 2 (Hilbert–Schmidt operator and eigenvalues) . Let (W, P ) be a probability space and 

h : W2 → R be a symmetric kernel function with E[h(W1, W 2)2] < ∞. The Hilbert–Schmidt operator Th : L2(P ) → L2(P ) associated with h is defined as 

(Thf )( w) = E[h(w, W ′) f (W ′)] , f ∈ L2(P ),

where W ′ ∼ P is an independent copy of W .An eigenvalue–eigenfunction pair (λ, ϕ ) of Th satisfies 

(Thϕ)( w) = λ ϕ (w), ϕ ∈ L2(P ), ϕ ̸ ≡ 0.

The collection {λr}r≥1 of (possibly infinitely many) eigenvalues is referred to as the spectrum of 

Th.

The asymptotic distribution differs between degenerate cases and non-degenerate cases, as shown below by 1. Under degenerate cases, the limiting distribution would be a mixture of chi-square distributions which . 

Lemma 1 (Asymptotic distribution of degenerate U -Statistics, Atta-Asiamah and Yuan 2019, Vaart [1998]) . Let Un be a first-order degenerate U -statistic of order 2 with E[Un] = θ. Then,

# B Test Statistic and Its U -statistic Representation in the Discrete Regime 

The next section develops the asymptotic null theory for ∆ n in the fully discrete regime, where both Z and X take values in finite sets and the composite neighborhoods Ci and Fi are obtained by exact matching: Ci = {j̸ = i : Zj = Zi} and Fi = {j̸ = i : ( Zj , X j ) = ( Zi, X i)}. In this regime, no local-polynomial debiasing is required: exact matching introduces no smoothing bias, and the raw statistic ∆ n itself converges in distribution to a mixture of chi-squared random variables under 

H0. The present appendix sets up the U -statistic representation driving this result and records the population-level interpretation of ∆ n as a kernel-based conditional coefficient of determination. K is bounded and normalized with 0 ≤ K(y, y ′) ≤ 1 and K(y, y ) = 1; for discrete Y this holds for the indicator kernel, and for continuous Y it is satisfied by the Gaussian RBF. 

Proposition 6 (Properties of ∆ norm in the discrete regime) . Under the assumption of a normal-ized kernel, ∆norm satisfies: 1. Boundedness: 0 ≤ ∆norm ≤ 1 asymptotically. 2. Full dependence: If Y is a deterministic function of (X, Z), then ∆norm → 1.3. Independence: If Y ⊥⊥ X | Z, then ∆norm → 0.Proof. (1) Boundedness. Since K(Yi, Y j ) ≤ 1 we have QZ ≤ 1, so the denominator is nonnega-tive. By monotonicity, conditioning on the finer stratification ( Z, X ) cannot decrease the within-19 stratum similarity: QZ,X ≥ Q Z , hence ∆ norm ≥ 0. Also QZ,X ≤ 1 implies QZ,X − Q Z ≤ 1 − Q Z ,so ∆ norm ≤ 1. 

(2) Full dependence. If Y = f (X, Z), then within any ( Z, X ) stratum all observations share the same value of Y , giving K(Yi, Y j ) = 1 for every j ∈ F i and QZ,X → 1. Substituting yields ∆norm = (1 − Q Z )/(1 − Q Z ) = 1. 

(3) Independence. Under H0, the within-stratum conditional law of Y is invariant under further conditioning on X: the ( Z, X ) and Z strata produce the same conditional distribution of 

Y . The within-stratum kernel means coincide in expectation, E[QZ,X ] = E[QZ ], giving QZ,X −QZ → 0 and ∆ norm → 0. This interpretation justifies ∆ norm as a kernel-based conditional R2: the proportion of within-coarse-stratum variability of Y explained by further conditioning on X. The unnormalized ∆ n

inherits the same sign and convergence properties and is the basis for inference; we retain it because its U -statistic form makes the asymptotic null distribution explicit. 

# D Proofs of Regime I – All Discrete Case 

In this section, we establish the asymptotic null distribution of ∆ norm when all variables ( X, Y, Z )are discrete. The key insight is that under H0, the test statistic can be represented as a first-order degenerate U -statistic, whose limiting distribution is a weighted sum of independent chi-squared random variables.

## D.2 Proof Strategy 

The proof proceeds in three main steps: (I). U -statistic representation: We express ∆ n as a symmetric order-2 U -statistic with kernel function h(( Zi, X i, Y i), (Zj , X j , Y j )). (II). First-order degeneracy: We prove that under H0, the kernel h satisfies 

E[h(( Zi, X i, Y i), (Zj , X j , Y j )) | (Zj , X j , Y j )] = 0 almost surely ,

which implies that Var[ E[h(( Zi, X i, Y i), (Zj , X j , Y j )) | (Zj , X j , Y j )]] = 0, i.e., first-order degeneracy. (III). Application of limiting theory: We invoke Lemma 1 and apply Slutsky’s theorem to obtain the mixture-of-chi-squared limit.

## D.4 Step 2: First-Order Degeneracy Under H0

To apply Lemma 1, we must establish that under H0, the kernel h is first-order degenerate. By Definition 1, this requires showing Var [E[h(( Zi, X i, Y i), (Zj , X j , Y j )) | (Zj , X j , Y j )] ] = 0 . (38) This condition holds if and only if E[h(( Zi, X i, Y i), (Zj , X j , Y j )) | (Zj , X j , Y j )] is constant almost surely. We will prove that this conditional expectation equals zero almost surely under H0.

Remark 8. The key insight in the proof is that under H0, the refinement from Z-neighborhoods to (Z, X )-neighborhoods provides no additional information about Y -agreement beyond what is already captured by Z-neighborhoods alone. This is formalized through the cancellation in (61) ,which occurs precisely because of the conditional independence Y ⊥⊥ X | Z.

Remark 9. The data-dependent nature of the neighborhoods (through ci and fi) introduces com-plex dependencies between observations. However, Conditions (32) –(33) ensure that these degrees concentrate around their population analogs, allowing us to treat them as approximately deter-ministic in the asymptotic analysis. This is a key technical point that distinguishes our approach from classical U -statistic theory with fixed kernels. 

## D.6 Technical Lemmas 

For completeness, we state a technical lemma used in the proof. 

Lemma 2 (Conditional Independence Under i.i.d. Sampling) . Let {(Xi, Y i, Z i)}ni=1 be i.i.d. sam-ples from distribution P . For any measurable function ϕ : X × Y → R and any i̸ = j, we have 

ϕ(Xj , Y j ) ⊥⊥ Zi | Zj . (68) 

In particular, 

P (ϕ(Xj , Y j ) | Zi = Zj , Z j ) = P (ϕ(Xj , Y j ) | Zj ). (69) 

Proof. This follows immediately from the i.i.d. assumption: ( Xj , Y j , Z j ) and Zi are independent random variables, hence ( Xj , Y j ) and Zi are conditionally independent given Zj . The second statement follows by taking ϕ to be the indicator of any measurable event. 

## D.7 Extension to High-Dimensional Discrete Conditioning 

The derivation above assumes Z is a univariate discrete variable, but the logic extends naturally to the case where Z is a high-dimensional random vector Z = ( Z(1) , . . . , Z (d)) ∈ Z d with countable support. In this multivariate setting, the neighborhoods Ci and Fi are defined by the intersection of exact matches across all dimensions of Z (and X). The validity of Theorem 7 in high dimensions relies on the “fixed support” asymptotic regime. Specifically, provided that the joint support of ( Z, X) remains fixed while n → ∞ , the counts in each occupied cell will eventually satisfy the divergence conditions cip

−→ ∞ and fip

−→ ∞ . Under these conditions, the concentration of the empirical degrees ci/n to the population masses pZ(z)holds regardless of the dimension d.However, it is important to note that for finite sample sizes, the “curse of dimensionality” may cause many cells to be empty or sparsely populated if d is large relative to log n. In such regimes, the convergence rates in (33) may slow down, but the asymptotic degeneracy of the 

U -statistic kernel—which drives the null distribution—remains a structural property of the con-ditional independence hypothesis Y ⊥⊥ X | Z, invariant to the dimensionality of the conditioning set. 

# E Proofs of Regime I – Y continuous Case 

In this section, we establish the asymptotic distribution of ∆ n when variables X and Z are discrete, but Y are continuous. The main difference between this scenario and Appendix D lies in the choice of kernel function K. As Y is continuous, we employ a general bounded positive definite kernel K : Y × Y → R (e.g., the Gaussian kernel K(y, y ′) = exp( −∥ y − y′∥2/2σ2)) instead of the exact matching indicator. The neighborhoods for Z and ( Z, X ) remain defined by exact matching on the discrete covariates as in the original formulation. 26 E.1 Main Result

## E.2 Proof Strategy 

The derivation of the asymptotic distribution for the continuous-Y regime follows the same archi-tectural framework as the all-discrete case, relying on the theory of degenerate U -statistics. The structural decomposition of the test statistic based on discrete Z-strata remains invariant. The primary modification lies in the substitution of the exact matching indicator with a continuous kernel function. The proof proceeds in three steps: (I). Kernelized U -statistic Representation: We generalize the representation of ∆ n by replacing the discrete Dirac kernel 1(Yi = Yj ) with a bounded positive definite kernel K(Yi, Y j ). We show that despite this substitution, the statistic retains the form of a symmetric order-2 U -statistic, denoted as U Kn , whose weights depend solely on the discrete cell counts of (Z, X ). (II). Degeneracy via Conditional Integration: We establish the first-order degeneracy of the modified kernel under H0:

E[hK (( Zi, X i, Y i), (Zj , X j , Y j )) | (Zj , X j , Y j )] = 0 almost surely .

(III). Spectral Limit using Integral Operators: With degeneracy established, we invoke the asymp-totic theory for U -statistics with general kernels. The limiting distribution remains a weighted sum of chi-squared variables, ∑ λr(Z2 

> r

− 1). The key distinction is that {λr}

are now eigenvalues of a Hilbert–Schmidt integral operator acting on L2(Y), rather than a finite-dimensional matrix.

## E.6 Technical Lemmas 

For completeness, we state a technical lemma used in the proof. 

Lemma 3 (Indicator decomposition of conditional expectation) . Let (Ω , F, P) be a probability space. Let A ∈ F be an event, B an integrable random variable, and G ⊆ F a sub-σ-algebra. Define the conditional probability P(A | G ) and the conditional expectation on the event by 

E[B | A, G] := 



E[B1A | G ]

P(A | G ) , on {P(A | G ) > 0},

0, on {P(A | G ) = 0 }.

30 Then almost surely 

E[1A B | G ] = P(A | G ) E[B | A, G]. (91) 

Proof. By the definition given above, on the set {P(A | G ) > 0} we have 

E[B1A | G ] = P(A | G ) · E[B1A | G ]

P(A | G ) = P(A | G ) E[B | A, G].

On the complementary set {P(A | G ) = 0 }, we have E[B1A | G ] = 0 by the defining property of conditional expectation (since B1A = 0 a.s. conditional on G there), and the right-hand side is also 0 by the definition of E[B | A, G] on this set. Hence, the equality (91) holds almost surely. 

# F Asymptotic Theory in Regime II: The All-Continuous Case

## F.1 The problem with the raw statistic and the role of debiasing 

The statistic (92) is not a fixed-kernel U -statistic. The events j ∈ F i and j ∈ C i depend on the entire sample through rank operations on ( Z, X )- and Z-space respectively. The appropriate asymptotic framework is therefore that of stabilizing geometric graph functionals in the tradition of Penrose and Yukich [2001], Penrose [2003b], and Last and Penrose [2018], adapted to growing 

kn via rescaled stabilization. A direct analysis of ∆ n under this framework yields a Gaussian limit at the √n scale, √n(∆ n −

θn) ⇒ N (0 , τ 2graph ), where θn := E[∆ n] is the null centering and τ 2graph is the overlap-variance constant. However, the centering θn presents a fundamental practical obstacle: even under H0,

θn does not vanish exactly. The Taylor-expansion argument detailed in Section F.13 establishes 

θn = O(( kn/n )2/D ), so θn → 0 as n → ∞ , but the question of whether the rescaled centering √nθ n remains controlled at the inferential scale is more delicate. For kn = nα,

√n θ n ≍ n1/2−2(1 −α)/D = n1/2−2/D +2 α/D ,

which diverges whenever α > 1 − D/ 4, i.e., for any α > 0 when D ≥ 4 and for moderately large 

α in intermediate dimensions. This divergent bias makes the test statistic √n∆n unsuitable for direct hypothesis testing without either (i) explicit bias correction or (ii) a calibration scheme that absorbs the bias internally. We adopt the local-polynomial debiasing approach of (i): we replace each of the two simple inner averages by a local-polynomial regression intercept of degree p. The polynomial reproduc-tion identities then annihilate the smoothing bias up to order p, and under a corresponding bias killing condition on kn, the rescaled centering of the debiased statistic satisfies √nθ (p)

## F.3 Standing assumptions 

We work throughout under the following assumptions, which combine smoothness, null struc-ture, kernel boundedness, growth rate of kn, and a dimensional condition required for the strict positivity of the limiting variance. 

Assumption 1 (Smooth continuous model) . The random vector (Z, X, Y ) ∈ RdZ × RdX × Y 

admits a joint density with respect to an appropriate product measure. The marginal densities 

fZ and fZ,X are supported on compact convex sets SZ ⊂ RdZ and SZ,X ⊂ RD with C2 boundary, are bounded above and below by positive constants f , f on their respective supports, and are Cp+1 

with uniformly bounded derivatives up to order p + 1 .

32 Assumption 2 (Null hypothesis and continuous mark law) . Under H0, Y ⊥⊥ X | Z. The conditional law PY |Z=z varies continuously in z against bounded test functions, and the conditional kernel mean 

κ(y, z ) := E[K(y, Y ′) | Z′ = z] (99) 

is Cp+1 in z with derivatives uniformly bounded in y.

Assumption 3 (Kernel regularity) . The kernel K : Y × Y → R is bounded and symmetric: 

sup 

> y,y ′∈Y

|K(y, y ′)| ≤ MK < ∞, K(y, y ′) = K(y′, y ).

Assumption 4 (Growth of kn). The sequence kn satisfies 

kn → ∞ , kn/n → 0, kn/(Np log n) → ∞ .

The last condition strengthens the standard kn/ log n → ∞ in order to handle uniform convergence of the local design matrices over n anchors and N 2 

> p

matrix entries. 

Assumption 5 (Dimensional non-degeneracy) . dX ≥ 1.

Assumption 5 is essential for the strict positivity of the limiting overlap variance τ 2 

> p

. When 

dX = 0, the fine and coarse neighborhoods coincide in distribution and the statistic is identically zero; this trivial case is excluded.

Rescaled exponential stabilization (Section F.8). The debiased local score depends on Wn

## F.6 Local Poisson approximation and uniform NN-radius control 

The local Poisson approximation is the foundation for both the stabilization analysis and the overlap variance calculations to follow. It expresses the fact that, after rescaling by the appro-priate local NN radius, the empirical point process in a shrinking neighborhood of an anchor converges to a homogeneous Poisson point process. The uniform NN-radius concentration result quantifies the rate at which the random NN radii concentrate around their population analogues. 

Lemma 4 (Local Poisson approximation) . Under Assumption 1, fix t0 = ( z0, x 0) in the interior of SZ,X and write 

rF,n (t0) := (nf Z,X (t0))−1/D .

For disjoint Borel sets A1, . . . , A m with |∂A ℓ| = 0, the vector of counts is multinomial with cell probabilities all tending to zero at rate n−1, hence converges to independent Poisson random variables with means |Aℓ|. ## F.8 Rescaled exponential stabilization 

The classical fixed-scale stabilization framework of Penrose and Yukich [2001] does not directly apply to the kn-nearest-neighbor graph when kn → ∞ , as observed by Bhattacharya [2019]. The reason is that the kn-NN ball has shrinking diameter in the original metric and cannot be stabilized by a fixed radius. The remedy (similar in spirit with Penrose [2003a]) is to work in the 

rescaled metric obtained by dividing by the local NN radius. In this rescaled metric, the kn-NN ball has bounded radius (in expectation), and stabilization holds with an exponential tail. 

Lemma 7 (Rescaled exponential stabilization) . Under Assumptions 1–4, for each anchor Wi,define the rescaled stabilization radius 

Rn(Wi, Wn) := max 



R(kn) 

> F,n

(Zi, X i)

ρF (Zi, X i) , R(kn) 

> C,n

(Zi)

ρC (Zi)

 . (112) 

The local graph score ξn,p (Wi, Wn) defined in (100) is unchanged by modifications of Wn outside the union of the fine ball B(( Zi, X i), R n(Wi, Wn)ρF (Zi, X i)) and the coarse ball B(Zi, R n(Wi, Wn)ρC (Zi)) .Moreover, there exist constants C, c > 0 such that for all t ≥ 1,

sup 

> n

P {Rn(W1, Wn) > t } ≤ C exp (−c k n(t − 1) dZ ). (113) 

The same radius stabilizes the centered score ζn,p (Wi, Wn) defined in (104) , since mn,p (Wi) de-pends on Wi and the law P only. Proof. By the definition of ξn,p in (100)–(97), the fine intercept ̂ a(p) 

> i,F

(Zi, X i) of ( Zi, X i) in ( Z, X )-space, by the defining property of the kn-NN set. The same property gives the analogous statement for ̂ a(p)

37 for t ≥ 1 + ε (the bound is a Chernoff-type bound, with exponent ≍ kn(tD − 1) 2/t D from (111) which for t = 1 + η small is ≍ knη2, while for t large is ≍ kntD; we have used the standard interpolated bound kn(t − 1) 2 ∧ kntD ≥ kn(t − 1) D for the unified statement). Similarly,

Since D = dZ +dX ≥ dZ , the coarse side governs the slower decay, and (113) follows by combining the two bounds via the union bound. The same argument applies to ζn,p since mn,p (Wi) is a deterministic function of Wi alone, independent of the external sample configuration. 

Remark 12 (Why rescaling matters) . The original kn-NN ball has Euclidean radius ρF =

O(( kn/n )1/D ), which vanishes as n → ∞ . Without rescaling, the stabilization radius would inherit this shrinkage, and any fixed-scale stabilization condition such as sup n P (R > t ) ≤ Ce −ct 

for a stabilization radius R measured in the original metric would fail trivially: R itself is o(1) 

rather than O(1) . The Penrose–Yukich framework applied to fixed-radius geometric functionals (e.g., the k-NN graph for fixed k) is therefore not directly applicable, as noted by Bhattacharya [2019]. The rescaled stabilization, by contrast, measures the stabilization radius in units of ρF or ρC .In these units, the kn-NN ball has radius 1 in expectation (under the calibration of ρF and ρC

as population radii), and the deviations R(kn) 

> F,n

/ρ F concentrate around 1 with Chernoff-type tails. This is the correct adaptation of the stabilization framework to the growing-kn regime, and it is the scaling under which the proof of the stabilization CLT (Section F.22) proceeds.

Remark 13 (On the conditional moment bound) . A sharper conditional moment bound is avail-able and will be useful in the variance calculation of Section F.20. This bound is what will be used in Step 6 of the proof of Lemma 18. 

## F.13 Bias cancellation via polynomial reproduction

## F.15 The bias killing condition 

The bias bound (127) controls the unconditional mean θ(p) 

> n

, but to use √n∆(p) 

> n

as a test statistic without explicit bias correction we require the rescaled centering √n θ (p) 

> n

to be asymptotically negligible. This produces the bias killing condition 

√n 

> (

kn

n

> )(p+1) /D

→ 0, (136) which constrains the rate of growth of kn in terms of the dimension D and the local-polynomial order p.

Lemma 14 (bias killing in polynomial scale) . Suppose kn = nα for some α ∈ (0 , 1) . Then (136) 

holds if and only if 

α < 1 − D

2( p + 1) . (137) 

Proof. √n(kn/n )(p+1) /D = n1/2+( p+1)( α−1) /D → 0 iff 12 + (p+1)( α−1)  

> D

< 0. Solving for α yields (137). The admissible range of α in (137) depends jointly on the curse of dimensionality (large D)and the polynomial order (large p). For each D, the polynomial order p can be increased to expand the admissible range: 

• For p = 0 (no debiasing): α < 1 − D/ 2, which is empty for D ≥ 2. The raw statistic admits no valid bandwidth in dimensions D ≥ 2. 

• For p = 1 (linear debiasing): α < 1 − D/ 4. Admissible when D = 1 ( α < 3/4), D = 2 (α < 1/2), D = 3 ( α < 1/4). Empty for D ≥ 4. 

• For p = 2 (quadratic debiasing): α < 1 − D/ 6. Admissible when D ≤ 5. 

• For general p and D ≥ 1: (137) admits a nonempty range of α ∈ (0 , 1) if and only if 

p ≥ D/ 2. The minimal polynomial order required for valid inference in dimension D is therefore p∗(D) := ⌈D/ 2⌉.

Remark 14 (Tradeoff with growth of Np). Increasing p to satisfy (137) comes at a cost: the dimension of the local-polynomial design matrix is Np = (dZ +pp

> )

, which grows polynomially in p.The growth condition kn/(Np log n) → ∞ in Assumption 4 then forces 

kn ≫

> (

dZ + pp

> )

log n, 

which is a much stronger condition than the standard kn/ log n → ∞ when p is large. The practical implication is that inference becomes more demanding (requires larger kn) in higher dimensions, both via the bias killing constraint (137) and via the design-regularity constraint kn ≫ Np log n.A natural choice is p = ⌈D/ 2⌉, which is the smallest polynomial order admitting a valid bias killing bandwidth. For this choice, Np ≍ dD/ 2 

> Z

, which is manageable in moderate dimensions but grows rapidly. The smoothness condition κ ∈ Cp+1 in Assumption 2 similarly becomes more demanding. 

Remark 15 (Bias correction without bias killing) . An alternative to bias killing is to estimate 

θ(p) 

> n

directly from the data and use √n(∆ (p) 

> n

−̂ θ(p) 

> n

) as the test statistic. If ̂ θ(p) 

> n

is a consistent 

44 estimator with √n(̂θ(p) 

> n

− θ(p) 

> n

) = op(1) , the resulting test has the same Gaussian limit at the √n

scale regardless of whether (137) holds. One implementation uses sample splitting: split the sample into halves S1 and S2. Com-pute the local-polynomial intercepts on S1, and use the explicit Taylor expansion of mn,p from Lemma 12 plus plug-in estimators of the bias coefficients (involving high-order derivatives of κ

and log-densities) computed on S2. The cross-fitted version satisfies the required rate condition under standard regularity. The trade-off is that this introduces additional smoothing parameters and computational overhead. In the remainder of this section we adopt the bias killing approach (137) for simplicity. 

## F.16 Overlap covariance: setup 

The variance of G(p) 

> n

decomposes by exchangeability into a diagonal and off-diagonal contribution: Var( G(p) 

> n

) = 1

n Var( ζ1,p ) + n − 1

n Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p ), (138) where ζi,p := ζn,p (Wi, Wn). By the conditional moment bound (125) from Remark 13, Var( ζ1,p ) = 

O(k−1 

> n

), so the diagonal contribution to Var( G(p) 

> n

> i,F

| Wi

> i,C

− E[̂ a(p) 

> i,C

| Wi

− Cov( ζ1,F,p , ζ 2,C,p ) − Cov( ζ1,C,p , ζ 2,F,p ). (141) We compute each of the four covariances and identify their leading n−1 contributions. For z ∈ S Z

and y1, y 2 ∈ Y , define the frozen mark covariance 

Γz (y1, y 2) := Cov ˜Y ∼PY |Z=z

(K(y1, ˜Y ), K (y2, ˜Y )). (142) Note that 

EY1,Y 2∼PY |Z=z [Γ z (Y1, Y 2)] = Var Y ∼PY |Z=z (κ(Y, z )) =: σ2(z), (143) by the iterated-variance formula: writing M ( ˜Y ) := E[K(Y, ˜Y )|Y ] with Y, ˜Y i.i.d. from PY |Z=z ,

EY1,Y 2 Γz (Y1, Y 2) = Cov ˜Y (M ( ˜Y ), M ( ˜Y )) = Var( M ( ˜Y )) = Var( κ(Y, z )). The function σ2(z) cap-tures the conditional-mark variance of K(Y1, Y 2) given Z1 = Z2 = z under H0, and is the key non-degeneracy quantity appearing in the variance formulas below.

## F.19 Fine–coarse overlap covariance

49 The strict positivity τ 2 

> p

> 0 requires the additional non-cancellation argument in Section F.21 and hinges on Assumption 5 ( dX ≥ 1) together with a primitive conditional-mark non-degeneracy condition. The variance limit (156) can in principle be zero in the degenerate case where the limiting fine and coarse fluctuation scores agree almost surely under the local Poisson Palm law, but this is excluded by the strict-positivity argument. 

## F.21 Strict positivity of τ 2

> p

We now establish the strict positivity of the overlap variance constant τ 2 

> p

= τ 2 

> F,p

+ τ 2 

> C,p

− 2τF C,p .This is the key non-degeneracy result for the limiting Gaussian distribution in Theorem 9. The mechanism is the dimensional mismatch between the fine ( D-dimensional) and coarse (dZ -dimensional) Palm processes: the fine Palm process contains Poisson points at {uX̸ = 0 } that the coarse process, being a Z-projection, cannot distinguish from points at uX = 0. Combined with the conditional-mark variance appearing in σ2(z), this produces an unmatchable component of the fine score, yielding τ 2 

> p

> 0. 

F.21.1 Primitive non-cancellation condition 

We replace the abstract requirement that the fine and coarse limiting fluctuation scores differ in the covariance seminorm by the following primitive condition. 

Condition 1 (Primitive non-cancellation) . There exists a measurable set A ⊆ S Z with PZ (A) > 0

such that for every z ∈ A :(I) The conditional law X | Z = z has a density fX|Z (·| z) with respect to Lebesgue mea-sure on RdX that is bounded below by a positive constant on some nonempty open ball 

BX (x0(z), δ (z)) ⊆ RdX .(II) The kernel detects nontrivial conditional variation of Y given Z = z:

σ2(z) = Var Y ∼PY |Z=z (κ(Y, z )) > 0. (157) Condition 1(I) is the geometric non-degeneracy condition. The X-coordinate has nonempty support in RdX conditional on Z = z, so the fine Palm process has genuine dX -dimensional spread. Condition (II) is the analytical non-degeneracy condition. The kernel K produces nontrivial fluctuation in κ(Y, z ) under the conditional law PY |Z=z . Both conditions are mild and checkable in concrete examples. 

F.21.2 Nontriviality of the fine equivalent kernel on an annulus 

The fine equivalent kernel ℓ(p) 

> F

is a polynomial of degree at most p on RdZ . By the population reproduction identity (116), ∫ ℓ(p) 

> F

dμ F = 1, so ℓ(p) 

> F

cannot vanish μF -almost everywhere. We now strengthen this to nontriviality on a specific region of BD(0 , 1) with X-extension.

{∥ uX ∥ ≥ η0} — i.e., EF contains D-dimensional points whose X-coordinate is bounded away from zero. This X-extension is what cannot be reproduced by the coarse Palm process, which sees only the Z-projection.

Step 4: Disjointness of the EF and GC contributions in the Palm limit. The crucial observation is that the coarse Palm process PC in RdZ is constructed at the rescaling ρC , distinct from the fine rescaling ρF . Concretely, the coarse kn-NN ball, in fine-rescaled coordinates, has radius 

εn = ρC /ρ F → 0 when dX ≥ 1 (Assumption 5). So in the fine-rescaled limit, the coarse field is generated entirely by Poisson points within the shrinking Z-region {∥ uZ ∥ ≤ εn}, which collapses to the origin. By contrast, the set EF from Lemma 19 is supported in {∥ uX ∥ ≥ η0} and has positive Lebesgue measure in RD. The Poisson points in EF are at distance ∥uX ∥ ≥ η0 > 0 from the 

Z-axis. In the fine-rescaled limit, these points are entirely disjoint from the support of the coarse field (which collapses to {u = 0 }). By the independence of Poisson increments on disjoint sets, the restriction PF |EF of the fine Palm process to EF is independent of GC . Therefore the orthogonal projection πC annihilates any contribution from PF |EF :

Remark 17. Assumption 5 enters at two places in the proof: 

(a) In Lemma 19, the construction of EF requires that the sections {uX : ∥(uZ , u X )∥ ≤ 1}

have positive dX -dimensional Lebesgue measure for uZ ∈ EZ . This is automatic when dX ≥ 1

but fails when dX = 0 .

(b) In Step 4 of the proof of Lemma 20, the disjointness of PF |EF and the coarse field GC in the Palm limit requires that the coarse Z-ball collapses to the Z-origin in fine-rescaled coordinates, which holds because εn = ρC /ρ F → 0 when dX ≥ 1. When dX = 0 , ρC = ρF and the coarse field is identical to the Z-projection of the fine field; no EF can be disjoint from it. When dX = 0 , the statistic ∆(p) 

> n

is in fact identically zero by construction (the fine and coarse neighborhoods coincide), and τ 2 

> p

= 0 trivially. Excluding this degenerate case is exactly the content of Assumption 5. 

Remark 18 (On the role of p). The strict positivity result is independent of the local-polynomial order p: for every p ≥ 0 (under the regularity assumptions), τ 2 

> p

> 0. The polynomial order p

affects the magnitude of τ 2 

> p

but not its positivity. Specifically, increasing p tends to increase the magnitude of ℓ(p) 

> F

on average (because higher-order polynomials have larger oscillations under the reproduction constraint), so τ 2 

> p

typically grows with p. The trade-off is that the variance of the test statistic also grows, and one’s effective sample size for estimating τ 2 

> p

shrinks. The optimal p

for testing power is therefore the minimal p that satisfies the bias killing constraint (137) , namely 

p = ⌈D/ 2⌉.

53 F.22 Stabilization CLT for the debiased graph fluctuation 

We now establish the central limit theorem for the centered graph fluctuation G(p) 

> n

. The proof combines the rescaled exponential stabilization of Lemma 7, the uniform moment bound of Lemma 11, the variance limit of Lemma 18, and the strict positivity τ 2

> 0 of Lemma 20, into the classical Penrose–Yukich stabilization framework adapted to growing kn via rescaling. 

Lemma 21 (Stabilization CLT for G(p)

## F.25 Extension to Heterogeneous Z

The derivation above assumes Z is a purely continuous variable, but the asymptotic distri-bution could be extended naturally to the case where Z is heterogeneous denoted as Z =(Zd1 , . . . , Z dp , Z c1 , . . . , Z cq ) ∈ Z dp+cq with countable support. Specifically, we partition the 56 sample into strata defined by the discrete components ( Zd1 , . . . , Z dp ). Within each stratum 

Sk = {i : ( Zd1 

> i

, . . . , Z dp 

> i

) = zdk }, the continuous components ( Zc1 , . . . , Z cq ) are handled via k-NN matching as in the purely continuous case. As the discrete columns of Z provide no geometric bias to ∆ n, we could condition on the dis-crete components ( Zd1 , . . . , Z dp ) and restrict attention to the continuous components ( Zc1 , . . . , Z cq )within each induced stratum. Since the discrete columns only determine the partition of the sam-ple into strata but do not enter the k-NN geometry, the asymptotic analysis within each stratum reduces to the purely continuous case. The overall asymptotic distribution of ∆ n then follows standard Theorem 9, which yields a normal distribution. 

# G Asymptotic Theory in Regime III: X Discrete, Z Con-tinuous 

In this section we develop the null limit theory for a local-polynomial debiased version of the sample-dependent kn-nearest-neighbor statistic in the mixed setting: X takes values in a finite set, and Z is continuous in RdZ . The underlying raw statistic is ∆n = 1

Ci = KNN kn

P denote the joint law of W = ( Z, X, Y ) on RdZ × { x(1) , . . . , x (M )} × Y , with M ≥ 2 and stratum probabilities pm := P (X = x(m)) ∈ (0 , 1) for each m.

## G.1 Structural difference from the all-continuous case 

The structural distinction between Regimes II and III is the effective sample size contributing to each neighborhood. Both Fi and Ci are constructed by kn-NN search in the dZ -dimensional 

Z-space, so the geometry is intrinsically dZ -dimensional throughout. The fine neighborhood is restricted to a stratum of expected size np m, while the coarse neighborhood uses all n points. The corresponding population radii (defined below) satisfy 

ρZ|m(z) > ρ Z (z) whenever pm < 1,

irrespective of whether X and Z are independent . This radius mismatch replaces the dimensional mismatch ( D > d Z ) of the all-continuous case as the structural feature driving the non-vanishing overlap variance. Three concrete implications follow: 

(i) Lower-dimensional curse of dimensionality. Both neighborhoods are dZ -dimensional, so the bias scales like ( kn/n )2/d Z rather than ( kn/n )2/D . The discrete X contributes no smoothing dimension, so the inferential problem is genuinely easier in moderate dZ .

(ii) Cleaner non-cancellation mechanism. Strict positivity of the limiting graph variance τ 2

> p

requires only pm < 1 for some m together with conditional-mark non-degeneracy. No higher-order Palm-process argument is needed; the radius mismatch produces an explicit positive contribution from the annulus between the coarse and fine balls. 

(iii) Same overall framework. The decomposition √n ∆(p) 

> n

= √n θ (p) 

> n

+ √n L (p) 

> n

+ √n G (p) 

> n

and the role of local-polynomial debiasing in killing the leading bias are identical to the all-continuous 57 case. The stabilization CLT, the strict positivity argument, and the testing procedure all parallel Regime II with the appropriate modifications recorded below.

## G.3 Standing assumptions 

Assumption 6 (Mixed continuous-discrete model) . The X-marginal is supported on the finite set {x(1) , . . . , x (M )} with M ≥ 2 and pm := P (X = x(m)) ∈ (0 , 1) for every m. For each m, the conditional law Z | X = x(m) has density fZ|X (·| x(m)) supported on a compact set Zm ⊂ RdZ with 

C2 boundary, bounded above and below by positive constants f , f on Zm, and Cp+1 with uniformly bounded derivatives up to order p + 1 . The marginal density fZ (z) = ∑ 

> m

pmfZ|X (z|x(m)) inherits the same regularity on ⋃ 

> m

Zm.

Assumption 7 (Null hypothesis and continuous mark law) . Under H0, Y ⊥⊥ X | Z. The conditional law PY |Z=z varies continuously in z against bounded test functions, and the conditional 

58 kernel mean 

κ(y, z ) := E[K(y, Y ′) | Z′ = z]

is Cp+1 in z with derivatives uniformly bounded in y. Under H0, E[K(y, Y ′) | Z′ = z, X ′ = x] = 

κ(y, z ) does not depend on x.

Assumption 8 (Kernel regularity) . The kernel K : Y × Y → R is bounded and symmetric: 

sup y,y ′ |K(y, y ′)| ≤ MK < ∞.

Assumption 9 (Growth of kn). The sequence kn satisfies kn → ∞ , kn/n → 0, and kn/(Np log n) →∞.

The dimensional non-degeneracy assumption of the all-continuous case is replaced in Regime III by the requirement that X takes at least two values with strictly positive but not full mass. This is already encoded in Assumption 6 ( M ≥ 2 and pm ∈ (0 , 1)). No additional dimensional assumption on RdX is needed because X is discrete.

## G.5 Outline of the proof 

The remainder of this section establishes 

√n ∆(p) 

> n

⇒ N (0 , τ 2 

> p

), (183) under Assumptions 6–9, a primitive non-cancellation condition, and the bias killing condition √n(kn/n )(p+1) /d Z → 0. See Theorem 11 of Subsection G.21. The proof follows the same five ingredients as Regime II: 1. Local Poisson approximation and uniform kn-NN radius concentration in the stratum-aware setting (Section G.6). The fine Palm process is the stratum-m thinning of the full Palm process at rate pm.2. Rescaled exponential stabilization (Section G.7). The score is stabilized by the maximum of the rescaled fine and coarse kn-NN radii, both in the dZ -dimensional metric. 59 3. Uniform local-polynomial design regularity (Section G.9). The fine and coarse design ma-trices both converge to the same population limit M (p) 

> C

(the uniform-on-ball moment matrix in RdZ ), since both the stratum and full populations are uniform on the dZ -ball after rescal-ing. This is a structural simplification compared to Regime II, where the fine and coarse population matrices differ. 4. Bias and projection negligibility (Section G.11). The polynomial reproduction identity gives 

θ(p) 

> n

= O(( kn/n )(p+1) /d Z ) and a2 

> n,p

= O(( kn/n )2( p+1) /d Z ), both improved by a factor of dZ in the exponent compared to Regime II’s D in the denominator. 5. Overlap variance and stabilization CLT (Sections G.14 onward). The variance decomposes as τ 2 

> p

= τ 2 

> F,p

+ τ 2 

> C,p

− 2τF C,p . Strict positivity is proved via the radius mismatch λm > 1 and a direct annulus argument: the fine ball contains stratum-m Palm points in the annulus 

{1 < ∥u∥ ≤ λm} in coarse-rescaled coordinates that the coarse score cannot see. The technical implementation of these steps in Regime III parallels Regime II closely. Through-out the remainder of this section we will, where the modifications are routine, state the relevant lemma and indicate the difference from the corresponding Regime II proof rather than redoing the calculation. The genuinely novel arguments — primarily the radius-mismatch non-cancellation in Section G.19 and the stratum-aware Poisson analysis in Section G.6 — are given in full. 

## G.6 Local Poisson approximation and uniform NN-radius control 

The geometric-probability foundations carry over from Regime II (Section F.6) with two modifi-cations: (i) the fine Palm process is restricted to the stratum {X = x(m)}, and (ii) both rescalings are intrinsically dZ -dimensional. 

Lemma 22 (Local Poisson approximation, mixed case) . Under Assumption 6, fix z0 ∈ int( Zm)

and m ∈ { 1, . . . , M }. Define the rescaled rates 

rF,n (z0, m ) := (np mfZ|X (z0|x(m)))−1/d Z , rC,n (z0) := (nf Z (z0))−1/d Z .

(which under H0 does not depend on X). Proof. The unrestricted statement is identical to the Z-projection part of Lemma 4 applied to the marginal density fZ . For the stratum-m statement, the number of stratum points is 

Nm ∼ Binomial( n, p m) with Nm/n → pm a.s. Condition on Nm, the stratum-m Z -coordinates are i.i.d. with density fZ|X (·| x(m)). Applied to the stratum with effective sample size Nm and density fZ|X (·| x(m)), the binomial-to-Poisson argument of Lemma 4 gives that the rescaled stra-tum process at rate rF,n (z0, m ) converges to a homogeneous unit-intensity Poisson process. The fluctuation of Nm around np m contributes an O(n−1/2) correction that is absorbed in the (1+ o(1)) density factor. The marked statement follows from Assumption 7: under H0, the conditional law of Yj

given ( Zj , X j ) = ( zj , x (m)) is PY |Z=zj , which converges to PY |Z=z0 uniformly as zj → z0 by the continuity of z 7 → PY |Z=z .60 Remark 21 (Stratum thinning interpretation) . The fine Palm process P∞,m F can equivalently be viewed as a thinning of the full unit-intensity Palm process by the stratum-membership indicator 

1{X = x(m)}, with thinning probability pmfZ|X (z0|x(m))/f Z (z0). After re-calibrating the rate by 

rF,n (z0, m )/r C,n (z0) = ( fZ (z0)/(pmfZ|X (z0|x(m)))) 1/d Z = λm(z0), the resulting process has unit intensity in fine-rescaled coordinates. This thinning interpretation underlies the radius mismatch 

ρZ|m > ρ Z when pm < 1: thinning reduces the local intensity, so a larger radius is needed to capture kn stratum points.

The fine and coarse Palm pro-cesses in Regime III are distinct random objects, but they live in the same dimension RdZ and share the underlying sample. In coarse-rescaled coordinates (units of ρZ (z0)), the fine Palm pro-cess P∞,m F is the stratum-m thinning of the full Palm process at rate pm, viewed at the dilated scale

## G.8 Population moment matrices and equivalent kernels 

The local-polynomial infrastructure simplifies notably in Regime III relative to Regime II. Both the fine (stratum-m Palm) and coarse (full Palm) processes are unit-intensity Poisson processes in 

RdZ , after rescaling by their respective radii ρZ|m and ρZ . The corresponding limiting offset distri-butions are both uniform on the dZ -ball — the only difference is the rescaling unit. Consequently, the fine and coarse population moment matrices coincide. Let μC denote the uniform probability measure on BdZ (0 , 1). Define the single population moment matrix

Proof. The argument is the four-step proof of Lemma 9, with the following modification: on the fine side, the per-anchor Bernstein bound is applied within the stratum-mi Palm process rather than the full Palm process, and the union bound is taken over n anchors together with the M

strata (so over nM effective anchor-stratum pairs). Since M is fixed and finite, this enters as a constant factor and does not change the validity of the bound under Assumption 9. 62 The structural simplification is that the limit is the same matrix M (p) on both sides, because both rescaled Palm processes are uniform on the dZ -ball. By contrast, in Regime II the fine and coarse limits M (p) 

> F

, M (p) 

> C

differed (the fine limit was the Z-marginal of the uniform on BD(0 , 1), with non-uniform density ∝ (1 − ∥ u∥2)dX /2). The weight summability (193) follows from the boundedness of ( M (p))−1 and the unit-ball-bounded offsets, exactly as in Step 4 of the proof of Lemma 9.

## G.11 Bias cancellation via polynomial reproduction 

The bias analysis in Regime III parallels Lemma 12 in the all-continuous case, with both inner intercepts now smoothing over dZ -dimensional balls. The radii ρZ|m and ρZ have the same 

dimensional scaling ( kn/n )1/d Z but differ by the multiplicative factor λm(z) ∈ (1 , ∞) from (172); both contribute at the same order to the bias.

## G.13 The bias killing condition 

To use √n∆(p) 

> n

as a test statistic directly (without explicit bias correction), the rescaled centering

> n

√n

( kn

n

)(p+1) /d Z

→ 0. (201) 

Lemma 31 (bias killing in polynomial scale, mixed case) . Suppose kn = nα for some α ∈ (0 , 1) .Then (201) holds if and only if 

α < 1 − dZ

2( p + 1) . (202) 64 Proof. Identical to Lemma 14, with D replaced by dZ .The admissible range (202) is strictly wider than its all-continuous analogue, reflecting the absence of the X-dimension contribution to the smoothing dimensionality: 

• For p = 0: α < 1 − dZ /2, admissible for dZ = 1 ( α < 1/2) but empty for dZ ≥ 2. 

• For p = 1: α < 1 − dZ /4. Admissible for dZ ≤ 3. 

• For p = 2: α < 1 − dZ /6. Admissible for dZ ≤ 5. 

• For general p: (202) is nonempty if and only if p ≥ dZ /2, giving the minimal polynomial order p∗(dZ ) := ⌈dZ /2⌉.In comparison to Regime II, where the analogous threshold was p∗(D) = ⌈D/ 2⌉, the minimal polynomial order in Regime III is smaller (by ⌈dX /2⌉ in typical cases), and the admissible band-width range is correspondingly wider. This is the quantitative manifestation of the milder curse of dimensionality in Regime III. 

Remark 24 (Bandwidth-design tradeoff) . The growth condition kn/(Np log n) → ∞ in As-sumption 9, with Np = (dZ +pp

), becomes less stringent in Regime III because dZ < D . For the minimum-order choice p = ⌈dZ /2⌉, Np depends only on dZ , not on dX . The mixed-case statistic is therefore strictly easier to calibrate than its all-continuous analogue in any dimension.

## G.17 Fine–coarse overlap covariance 

The fine–coarse overlap is the most distinctive calculation in Regime III.
