Remark 26 (Simplifying observations) . A few structural observations about the fine–coarse over-lap: (a) When pm = 1 /M for all m and X ⊥ Z (so fZ|X (·| x(m)) = fZ for all m), the radius ratio sim-plifies to λm ≡ M 1/d Z , a constant. The geometric coefficient J (p) 

> F C,m

then becomes a deterministic function of M and dZ , computable in closed form. 

68 (b) For p = 0 , ℓ(0) (u) = 1 /μ C (BdZ (0 , 1)) = 1 on the unit ball, and the geometric kernel B(p)

> F C,m

(r; z)

reduces to the volume of B(0 , 1) ∩ B(r, λ m(z)) . The geometric constant J (0) 

> F C,m

(z) is then the average ball-intersection volume between the unit ball and a λm-ball at a uniformly distributed center. (c) The asymmetry of the formula (210) (where τ 2 

> F,p

and τ 2 

> C,p

both equal I(p) ∫ σ2fZ , but τF C,p 

has a J (p)

> F C,m

-dependent integrand) reflects the genuine asymmetry between the fine and coarse neighborhoods: the fine ball is larger (radius λm > 1 in coarse-rescaled units) and stratum-restricted, while the coarse ball is smaller and unrestricted. The non-trivial geometry of this asymmetry is exactly what produces the strict positivity of τ 2 

> p

in Section G.19.

## G.19 Strict positivity of τ 2 

> p

## via radius mismatch 

The strict positivity of τ 2 

> p

in Regime III is established by a direct annulus argument that exploits the radius mismatch λm(z) > 1. The argument is structurally cleaner than the dimensional-mismatch argument of Regime II (Section F.21). In the all-continuous case, the non-cancellation came from a positive- measure X-extension region EF in the higher-dimensional fine ball whereas in the mixed case, the non-cancellation comes from the explicit annulus 

Am(z) := {u ∈ RdZ : 1 < ∥u∥ ≤ λm(z)} (215) in coarse-rescaled coordinates. This annulus has positive dZ -volume 

|Am(z)| = vdZ

(λm(z)dZ − 1) > 0 (216) whenever λm(z) > 1, i.e., whenever pm < 1 — which holds automatically under Assumption 6. The fine Palm process contains stratum-m points in Am(z) which the coarse process, with support inside the unit ball, cannot see. 

G.19.1 Primitive non-cancellation condition Condition 2 (Primitive non-cancellation, mixed case) . There exists a measurable set A ⊆ ⋃ 

> m

Zm

with PZ (A) > 0 such that for every z ∈ A :(I) There exists m = m(z) ∈ { 1, . . . , M } such that λm(z) > 1, equivalently pmfZ|X (z|x(m)) <fZ (z).(II) The kernel detects nontrivial conditional variation: 

σ2(z) = Var Y ∼PY |Z=z (κ(Y, z )) > 0. (217) 69 Condition (I) is the radius-mismatch requirement. It is automatically satisfied under As-sumption 6: with M ≥ 2 and pm ∈ (0 , 1) for each m, the marginal-density identity fZ (z) = ∑ 

> ℓ

pℓfZ|X (z|x(ℓ)) implies that pmfZ|X (z|x(m)) < f Z (z) for every m at any z with fZ (z) > 0and at least one other stratum density positive at z (which holds on a set of full PZ -measure). Condition (II) is the mark non-degeneracy, identical to its all-continuous analogue (157). 

G.19.2 Strict positivity via the annulus contribution Lemma 36 (Strict positivity of τ 2 

> p

, mixed case) . Under Assumptions 6–9 and Condition 2, 

τ 2

Remark 27 (Comparison with the all-continuous strict-positivity proof) . Three structural dif-ferences between the mixed and all-continuous strict-positivity arguments: (a) Non-cancellation locus. In Regime II, the set EF was constructed as a positive-measure subset of BD(0 , 1) with X-extension ∥uX ∥ ≥ η0; the existence of EF required dX ≥ 1. In Regime III, the set is the explicit annulus Am(z) = {1 < ∥u∥ ≤ λm(z)} in coarse-rescaled coordinates, with positive volume guaranteed by λm(z) > 1, i.e., by pm < 1.(b) Disjointness mechanism. In Regime II, the disjointness of the fine-process restriction to EF

from the coarse field followed from the asymptotic collapse of the coarse ball to the Z-origin in fine-rescaled coordinates ( εn → 0). In Regime III, the disjointness is exact (not asymptotic): 

Am(z) is disjoint from the unit ball BdZ (0 , 1) where the coarse field is supported, in coarse-rescaled coordinates, simply by definition. (c) Quantitative lower bound. In Regime II, the lower bound was τ 2 

> p

≥ c20|EF | · PZ (A) · inf σ2.In Regime III, the analogous bound includes the radius-mismatch factor (λdZ 

> m

− 1) /λ dZ 

> m

, which grows with the non-degeneracy of X: when pm → 0 (rare stratum), λm → ∞ and the lower bound saturates near σ2(z); when pm → 1 (concentrated X), λm → 1 and the lower bound vanishes — consistent with the test losing power as X becomes degenerate.

The remaining steps which include uniform (2 + δ)-th moment control from Lemma 28, dependency-graph CLT of Chen and Shao [2004, Theorem 2.7], and de-Poissonization via bounded add-one cost, proceed identically to the all-continuous case.

## G.23 Comparison with the all-continuous case 

Remark 28 (Summary comparison) . The complete null limit theory for ∆(p) 

> n

in Regime III parallels Regime II structurally but differs in three substantive ways that make the mixed case strictly easier: (a) Lower-dimensional curse. The bias scales like (kn/n )(p+1) /d Z in Regime III versus (kn/n )(p+1) /D 

in Regime II. Since dZ < D = dZ + dX , the bias is smaller, the variance constant a2 

> n,p

shrinks 

73 faster, and the admissible bandwidth range α < 1 − dZ /(2( p + 1)) is strictly wider than its Regime II analogue. The minimal polynomial order p∗(dZ ) = ⌈dZ /2⌉ is smaller than p∗(D) = ⌈D/ 2⌉.(b) Cleaner non-cancellation mechanism. In Regime II, the strict positivity of τ 2 

> p

required the di-mensional mismatch D > d Z (Assumption 5) and an Itˆ o-isometry argument with asymptotic dis-jointness of fine and coarse processes (via εn = ρC /ρ F → 0). In Regime III, the non-cancellation arises from the exact disjointness of the annulus Am(z) = {1 < ∥u∥ ≤ λm(z)} from the unit ball where the coarse process is supported, with the annulus volume vdZ (λdZ 

> m

− 1) > 0 guaranteed by 

pm < 1.(c) Robustness to joint structure. The Regime III strict positivity holds under any non-degenerate discrete X (i.e., pm ∈ (0 , 1) for some m) and mark non-degeneracy ( σ2(z) > 0), regardless of whether X and Z are independent . This is unlike a naive intuition based on the raw statistic’s first projection (Remark 23), where the gradient-mismatch mechanism would vanish under X ⊥ Z.The radius mismatch persists, giving the test universal validity in mixed settings. (d) Same overall framework. The √n-scale Gaussian limit, the testing procedure via studentiza-tion, and the role of local-polynomial debiasing in killing the leading bias are identical to Regime II. The unified perspective is that of a single statistical procedure (local-polynomial-debiased kn-NN graph functional) producing the same calibrated null inference across regimes, with the regime-specific analyses differing only in the dimensional accounting and the precise form of the geometric constants. 

Remark 29 (A bridging view) . The two regimes can be viewed as the endpoints of a continuum of mixed-type designs. Regime II ( X continuous, dX ≥ 1) uses the dimensional structure of 

X to produce the overlap variance via higher-dimensional Poisson fluctuations. Regime III ( X

discrete, dX = 0 ) uses the partition structure of X to produce the same overlap variance via stratum thinning. Both mechanisms are unified by the observation that the fine neighborhood operates on an “effective” density that differs from the coarse marginal — either by a higher-dimensional density (Regime II) or by a stratum thinning (Regime III). In both cases, the local-polynomial debiasing scheme combined with the overlap-variance CLT produces an asymptotically exact √n-test of H0.A unified treatment of arbitrary mixed-type designs (some components of X continuous, oth-ers discrete) follows by combining the dimensional-mismatch mechanism of Regime II with the stratum-thinning mechanism of Regime III. The structural analysis is the same as in the present two regimes, with the local-polynomial design matrix defined over the continuous components of 

X and the stratum restriction operating over the discrete components. 

# H Asymptotic Theory in Regime IV: X Continuous, Z

# Discrete 

In this section we develop the null limit theory for a local-polynomial debiased version of the 

kn-nearest-neighbor statistic in the mixed setting where X is continuous in RdX and Z takes values in a finite set. As we explain below, this regime is structurally the simplest of the four: under H0, the smoothing bias of the fine local-polynomial intercept vanishes exactly , with no remainder, by an algebraic identity rather than a Taylor-expansion bound. Consequently, no bias killing condition on kn is required, and the debiased CLT holds at the √n scale for any kn = nα

with α ∈ (0 , 1). The underlying raw statistic is, as before, ∆n = 1

## H.1 Structural distinctness of Regime IV 

Three structural features distinguish Regime IV from the previous three regimes: 

(i) Exact Z-matching on the coarse side. The coarse set Ci uses exact equality on Z rather than nearest-neighbor matching. Consequently, the coarse intercept 1

nZi − 1

∑

> j∈C i

K(Yi, Y j )is a within-stratum sample mean of nZi − 1 i.i.d. terms (given Zi). It carries no smoothing bias in 

X or Z; under H0, its conditional expectation given Wi equals κ(Yi, Z i) := E[K(Yi, Y ′)|Z′ = Zi]exactly. 

(ii) Within-stratum reduction. The fine set Fi does kn-NN smoothing in X-space within the stra-tum {j : Zj = Zi}. Because Z is discrete, the strata are disjoint; the entire problem decomposes into L independent within-stratum sub-problems indexed by ℓ.

(iii) Identical-in-x kernel under H0. The defining feature of Regime IV is that under H0 : Y ⊥⊥

X | Z,

E[K(y, Y ′) | X′ = x, Z ′ = z(ℓ)] = E[K(y, Y ′) | Z′ = z(ℓ)] =: κ(y, z (ℓ)), (229) 

independent of x. The conditional kernel mean is a function of z alone within each stratum. Com-bined with the polynomial reproduction identity for the local-polynomial weights, this constancy yields exact bias cancellation, not merely O(ρp+1 ) bias reduction (see Lemma 38 below).

Remark 30 (Equivalent local-polynomial form for the coarse side) . The coarse intercept ̂ ai,C in 

(235) can be written as the local-polynomial intercept of degree zero on the neighborhood containing all stratum members. Formally, taking p = 0 and the design matrix Mi,C = 1 (a scalar) gives weights wij,C = 1 /(nZi − 1) . The coarse side does not benefit from higher-order polynomial fitting because it already has zero bias in X. Throughout the section we use the simple form (235) for clarity, but the local-polynomial-degree-zero interpretation makes (236) a unified instance of the general local-polynomial framework of Section F. 

## H.3 Standing assumptions 

Assumption 10 (Mixed continuous-discrete model, Regime IV) . Z is supported on the finite set 

{z(1) , . . . , z (L)} with L ≥ 2 and qℓ := P (Z = z(ℓ)) ∈ (0 , 1) for each ℓ. For each ℓ, the conditional law X | Z = z(ℓ) has density fX|Z (·| z(ℓ)) supported on a compact set Xℓ ⊂ RdX with C2 boundary, bounded above and below by positive constants, and Cp+1 with uniformly bounded derivatives up to order p + 1 .

Assumption 11 (Null hypothesis) . Under H0, Y ⊥⊥ X | Z. The conditional law PY |Z=z(ℓ) is a probability measure on Y for each ℓ. The kernel mean 

κ(y, z (ℓ)) := E[K(y, Y ′) | Z′ = z(ℓ)]

is bounded uniformly in y, ℓ .

Assumption 12 (Kernel regularity) . K : Y×Y → R is bounded and symmetric, sup y,y ′ |K(y, y ′)| ≤ 

MK < ∞.

Assumption 13 (Growth of kn). kn → ∞ , kn/n → 0, and kn/(Np log n) → ∞ .

Notably absent from this list: 

• No bias killing condition on kn.

• No smoothness condition on κ(y, ·) in x or z. The function κ(y, z (ℓ)) is a finite list of values, trivially C∞ in z.

• No regularity of ∇ log fX|Z beyond Assumption 10. 76 H.4 Local score decomposition

## H.5 Exact bias cancellation under H0

The defining technical feature of Regime IV is the following result: under H0, both intercepts have conditional expectation equal to κ(Yi, Z i) exactly , so mn,p (w) = 0 identically on the support. The bias does not have an O(ρp+1 ) remainder as in Regimes II and III; it is identically zero. This makes the debiased CLT considerably simpler than in the previous regimes. 

Lemma 38 (Exact bias cancellation) . Under Assumptions 10–13 and the null hypothesis H0 :

Y ⊥⊥ X | Z, on the event that M(p) 

> i,F

is invertible, 

mn,p (w) = 0 for every w = ( z(ℓ), x, y ) with ℓ = 1 , . . . , L. (239) 

Consequently, 

θ(p) 

> n

= 0 , a2 

> n,p

= Var( gn,p (W1)) = 0 . (240) 

Proof. Fix w = ( z(ℓ), x, y ). We compute E[̂ a(p)

Remark 31 (Why exact cancellation occurs) . Lemma 38 is the central simplification of Regime IV and warrants explanation. In Regimes II and III, the bias arose because the kernel κ(y, z ) was a nontrivial function of z that was being smoothed by the NN averaging; the smoothing produced a bias of order ρ2 proportional to the curvature of κ and the log-density gradient. In Regime IV, the discrete Z structure eliminates z-smoothing entirely (the coarse side uses exact Z-matching, the fine side stays within the stratum), so the only smoothing happens in X-space. Under H0,the kernel is constant in X within each stratum (Equation (229) ), so the X-smoothing trivially reproduces the constant. The bias cancellation is not a Taylor-expansion approximation; it is an algebraic identity given by the polynomial reproduction property applied to a constant function. The corollary √n L (p) 

> n

≡ 0 identically (not just op(1) ) further simplifies the asymptotic anal-ysis: only the graph fluctuation G(p) 

> n

contributes to the limiting variance. 

Remark 32 (No bias killing required) . Because θ(p) 

> n

= 0 identically rather than O(( kn/n )(p+1) /d X ),no bias killing condition α < 1 − dX /(2( p + 1)) is needed. The CLT holds for any kn = nα with 

α ∈ (0 , 1) , and the choice of polynomial order p is unconstrained by bias considerations. The choice p = 0 (raw kn-NN average) is valid here, unlike in Regimes II and III where p ≥ ⌈ D/ 2⌉

or ⌈dZ /2⌉ was required. 

## H.6 Outline of the proof 

In view of the exact bias cancellation, the rest of the analysis reduces to: (i) verifying within-stratum design regularity and moment bounds (Part H.7); (ii) computing the limiting overlap variance via the stratum-disjoint structure (Section H.10); and (iii) establishing the stabilization CLT and the main theorem (Section H.14). The key conceptual contrast with the previous regimes is that cross-stratum covariance is exactly zero : anchors i, j with Zi̸ = Zj contribute zero to Cov( ζi,p , ζ j,p ) because neither neighbor-hood ever crosses stratum boundaries. The overall variance is therefore a finite-mixture sum of within-stratum overlap variances, weighted by stratum mass: 

n Var( G(p) 

> n

) → σ2 

> p

:= 

> L∑
> ℓ=1

qℓ σ2

> ℓ,p

, (241) where σ2 

> ℓ,p

is the within-stratum overlap variance — a single-regime quantity governed by the same Itˆ o-isometry / stabilization machinery as in Section F. 

## H.7 Within-stratum geometric machinery 

The geometric-probability and design-regularity infrastructure transfers directly from Regime II (Sections F.6–F.12) applied within each stratum ℓ. The substitutions are D → dX (the fine ball is dX -dimensional, not D-dimensional), fZ,X (z, x ) → fX|Z (x|z(ℓ)) (the stratum density), and 

ρF (z, x ) → ρX (z(ℓ)) as in (230). In this part we record the relevant lemmas without redoing the proofs.

Remark 33 (Coarse side requires no stabilization) . The coarse intercept ̂ ai,C is a function of Wi

(through its stratum Zi) and the marks of all other stratum members; it has no nearest-neighbor structure to stabilize. The stabilization radius Rn(Wi, Wn) in (245) therefore reflects only the fine side. This is a notable simplification compared to the previous regimes, where the stabilization radius was the maximum of the fine and coarse rescaled radii. The coarse-side dependence on the full stratum is still ”stabilizing” in the loose sense that the coarse intercept is a sample mean over nZi ∼ nq Zi exchangeable terms; its fluctuation is of order n−1/2 by the standard CLT for sample means. We account for this in the overlap variance calculation (Section H.10) without needing an explicit stabilization radius.

## H.10 Overlap variance and cross-stratum independence 

The variance of G(p) 

> n

decomposes by exchangeability as Var( G(p) 

> n

) = 1

n Var( ζ1,p ) + n − 1

n Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p ). (254) By the conditional moment bound (253), Var( ζ1,p ) = O(k−1 

> n

) + O(n−1),

where the second term comes from the coarse-side sample-mean fluctuation. The diagonal con-tribution to Var( G(p) 

> n

) is therefore O(( nk n)−1) + O(n−2) = o(n−1); in particular, the coarse-side diagonal term, despite scaling as O(n−1) in Var( ζ1,p ), contributes O(n−2) to Var( G(p) 

> n

) after the 1/n normalization in (254) — hence negligible. The off-diagonal term Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p ) is generically of order n−1 and dominates. Crucially, this off-diagonal covariance has the following stratum structure: 

Lemma 45 (Cross-stratum vanishing of covariance) . Under Assumptions 10–11, if Z1̸ = Z2 then 

Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p ) = 0 exactly for every n.Proof. Suppose Z1 = z(ℓ) and Z2 = z(ℓ′) with ℓ̸ = ℓ′. The score ζ1,p is a function of W1 and the marks/positions of stratum-ℓ points only (both ̂ a(p)1,F and ̂ a1,C involve only stratum-ℓ mem-bers of Wn). Symmetrically, ζ2,p is a function of W2 and stratum-ℓ′ points only. Stratum-ℓ and stratum-ℓ′ are disjoint subsets of Wn (no observation can have Zj = z(ℓ) and Zj = z(ℓ′) simultane-ously). Conditional on the stratum assignment ( Zi)ni=1 , the within-stratum data are independent across strata: the X- and Y -coordinates of stratum-ℓ points are i.i.d. from the conditional law (X, Y ) | Z = z(ℓ), independently of the stratum-ℓ′ data. Therefore ζ1,p and ζ2,p are conditionally independent given the stratum assignment, hence their covariance is exactly zero. The cross-stratum covariance vanishes exactly , not merely asymptotically. Combined with the stratum structure, this reduces the overall variance to a finite-mixture sum. 

Lemma 46 (Decomposition of Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p ) by stratum) . Under Assumptions 10–13, 

Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p ) =

P (Z1 = z(ℓ), Z 2 = z(ℓ′)) Cov( ζ1,p , ζ 2,p | Z1, Z 2) + (centering term) .

Remark 35 (Why the coarse-coarse contribution does not vanish) . Unlike Regimes II and III, where the coarse-coarse overlap was a genuine NN-graph overlap of order 1/(nk n) per pair 

83 (matched against the fine-fine of the same order), the Regime IV coarse-coarse covariance Cov( ̂a1,C ,̂ a2,C )

is the covariance of two sample means within a common stratum, of order 1/n ℓ ≍ 1/n . This is exactly the same order as the fine-fine overlap, and the fine-coarse overlap matches both. For the constant I(p) to exceed 1 (so that σ2

## H.13 Strict positivity of σ2

> p

The strict positivity of σ2 

> p

reduces, by (258), to (i) I(p) > 1 and (ii) σ2(z(ℓ)) > 0 for at least one ℓ.Both are mild conditions; in particular, no annulus or Itˆ o-isometry argument is needed, in stark contrast to Regimes II and III. 

Condition 3 (Primitive non-cancellation, Regime IV) . I(p) > 1, and there exists at least one

For the present purpose we adopt Condition 3 as a primitive assumption and remark that it is satisfied in all standard cases of practical interest: dX ≤ 5 and any p ≥ 0, or dX ≥ 1 and any

Remark 36 (Behavior under alternatives, Regime IV) . Under H1, the conditional expectation of the kernel E[K(y, Y ′)|X′ = x, Z ′ = z(ℓ)] genuinely depends on x within at least one stratum 

ℓ, and the within-stratum local-polynomial debiased statistic ∆(p,ℓ ) 

> n

detects this dependence at the within-stratum CLT-rate n−1/2 

> ℓ

≍ n−1/2. Consequently, T (p) 

> n

→ ∞ in probability under any fixed alternative H1 with detectable within-stratum conditional dependence in at least one stratum. The test is consistent against all such fixed alternatives, and detects n−1/2-local alternatives. 

Remark 37 (Summary comparison across regimes) . The complete null limit theory for ∆(p) 

> n

in Regime IV parallels the previous regimes structurally but differs in three substantive ways that make it the simplest: 

(a) Exact bias cancellation. In Regimes II and III, the bias was O(( kn/n )(p+1) /D ) or O(( kn/n )(p+1) /d Z ),requiring an bias killing condition α < 1 − · /(2( p + 1)) on the bandwidth. In Regime IV, θ(p) 

> n

= 0 identically , by an algebraic identity from the polynomial reproduction property applied to a kernel constant in x. No bias killing is needed; any α ∈ (0 , 1) is admissible. 

(b) Cross-stratum vanishing covariance. The discrete Z structure makes cross-stratum covariance 

exactly zero, reducing the variance calculation to a finite-mixture sum of within-stratum variances. This avoids the more delicate annulus/Itˆ o arguments needed in Regimes II and III for strict positivity. 

(c) Coarse-coarse contribution. Unlike the previous regimes, where the coarse intercept was a NN-graph average with overlap covariance of order 1/(nk n) per pair, the Regime IV coarse intercept is a stratum sample mean with covariance of order 1/n ℓ per pair. This larger coarse contribution is what makes the fine–coarse and coarse–coarse pieces match in scale, yielding the clean Pythagorean form σ2 

> p

∝ I (p) − 1.The unifying observation across all four regimes is that the local-polynomial debiased kn-NN statistic produces an asymptotically exact √n-test under each mixed-type scenario, with regime-specific simplifications. Regime IV is the most tractable both theoretically and practically — no smoothness requirements beyond bounded conditional densities, no bias killing, no high-order curvature corrections. 

86 I Consistency of the Debiased Test under H1

In this section we establish consistency of the local-polynomial debiased test statistic ∆ (p) 

> n

against fixed alternatives. The result holds universally across all four regimes developed in Sections F– H, under the same regularity conditions used for the null limits and an additional smoothness condition on the conditional kernel mean. In particular, the debiased weights w(p) 

> ij,F

, w (p) 

> ij,C

defined in (96) are constructed entirely from the sample (via the empirical design matrices M(p) 

> i,F

, M(p) 

> i,C

), so the population density fZ,X never appears in the weights themselves. The local-polynomial machinery therefore serves as an implicit kernel-density-style normalization that converges to its population analog at a rate sufficient to guarantee consistency of ∆ (p) 

> n

.

## I.1 Population limit 

Let H be the reproducing kernel Hilbert space associated with the characteristic kernel K, with conditional mean embeddings 

μY ZX (z, x ) := E[K(Y, ·) | Z = z, X = x], (262) 

μY Z (z) := E[K(Y, ·) | Z = z]. (263) The population measure of conditional dependence is 

T (P ) := EZ,X 

> [

∥μY ZX (Z, X ) − μY Z (Z)∥2

> H
> ]

. (264) Since K is characteristic (Sriperumbudur et al., 2010, Theorem 7), T (P ) = 0 if and only if 

Y ⊥⊥ X | Z. Under H1, T (P ) > 0. The debiased statistic ∆ (p) 

> n

is the empirical analog of T (P ) when expressed via the kernel inner product: 

T (P ) = E[E[K(Y, Y ′) | Z, X, Z ′ = Z, X ′ = X]] − E[E[K(Y, Y ′) | Z, Z ′ = Z]] , (265) where ( Z, X, Y ) and ( Z′, X ′, Y ′) are i.i.d. The first term is the population analog of the fine inter-cept; the second of the coarse intercept. The local-polynomial weights estimate these conditional expectations consistently. 

## I.2 Assumptions for consistency 

Assumption 14 (Conditions for consistency) . Throughout this section we assume: (C1) The regime-specific Assumptions 1–4 (Regime II), or their analogs in Regimes I, III, IV, hold. (C2) The kernel K is bounded, symmetric, and characteristic. (C3) The conditional kernel mean ˜κ(y, z, x ) := E[K(y, Y ′)|Z′ = z, X ′ = x] is Cp+1 in (z, x ) on the interior of the support, with derivatives uniformly bounded in y. The corresponding coarse mean κ(y, z ) = E[K(y, Y ′)|Z′ = z] has the same smoothness in z.

Condition (C3) replaces the null smoothness condition on κ(y, z ) used in the null analysis. Under H1, ˜κ(y, z, x ) depends nontrivially on x, and its smoothness controls the bias of the fine local-polynomial intercept as an estimator of ˜κ.

## I.4 Remarks on the role of the empirical weights 

Remark 38 (Why local-polynomial weights work as a density-estimator substitute) . The local-polynomial weights w(p) 

> ij,F

in (96) are constructed from the empirical design matrix M(p) 

> i,F

, which itself depends only on the sample. No population density appears in their construction. The fine intercept ̂ a(p) 

> i,F

= ∑ 

> j

w(p) 

> ij,F

K(Yi, Y j ) can be viewed as a local-polynomial regression of K(Yi, ·)

on the design points (Zj , X j )j∈F i . By standard local-polynomial theory (Fan and Gijbels, 1996, Chapter 3), such a regression is consistent for the conditional mean ˜κ(Yi, Z i, X i) provided the bandwidth ρF vanishes and the effective sample size kn diverges — both of which are guaranteed by Assumption 4. 

88 Crucially, the design matrix convergence M(p) 

> i,F

→ M (p) 

> F

in Lemma 9 implicitly absorbs the population density variation. The empirical design provides automatic bandwidth-and-density adaptation, sidestepping the need for an explicit kernel density estimator in the weights. 

Remark 39 (Robustness to bandwidth choice in consistency) . The consistency result (266) 

holds under any kn satisfying Assumption 4 — that is, kn → ∞ and kn/n → 0 (plus the design-regularity condition kn/(Np log n) → ∞ ). Unlike the null limit theorem (Theorem 9), no bias killing constraint α < 1 − D/ (2( p + 1)) is needed for consistency. Bias under H1 is asymptotically negligible at any rate-correct bandwidth; only the null analysis requires the more delicate bias killing for the √n-scale centering. 

Remark 40 (Universal consistency across regimes) . The proof of Theorem 15 above was written for the all-continuous Regime II. The same conclusion holds in Regimes I, III, and IV with the obvious modifications: (Regime I, both discrete.) The intercepts become cell-wise sample means; the law of large numbers within cells gives consistency directly, with no smoothing bias to control. (Regime III, X discrete, Z continuous.) The fine intercept averages within the discrete stratum 

{j : Xj = Xi} via kn-NN in Z; the coarse intercept averages over all Z-neighbors. Both con-verge to their respective conditional expectations by the same Taylor + polynomial reproduction argument in Z-space. (Regime IV, X continuous, Z discrete.) The fine intercept averages within the discrete stratum 

{j : Zj = Zi} via kn-NN in X; the coarse intercept is the within-stratum sample mean. Both converge to their respective conditional expectations; under H1, ˜κ(Y, Z, X )̸ = κ(Y, Z ) in at least one stratum, so T (P ) > 0 and the test diverges as in the proof above. In every regime, the variance estimator ̂ σ2 

> p

is constructed from quantities that remain bounded in probability under H1 (since the conditional kernel variance σ2 is bounded by ∥K∥2

> ∞

), so the test divergence (267) holds universally.

## J.1 Local alternatives and signal expansion 

We consider the contiguous mixture family 

fn(z, x, y ) := (1 − rn) fZ (z)fX|Z (x|z)fY |Z (y|z) + rn g(z, x, y ), rn → 0, (269) where fZ (z)fX|Z (x|z)fY |Z (y|z) is the null component (under which Y ⊥⊥ X | Z) and g is an alternative density that preserves the ( Z, X )-marginal: ∫ g(z, x, y ) dy = fZ (z)fX|Z (x|z). Under (269), the conditional independence hypothesis fails by an rn-perturbation, and the test’s power depends on whether rn exceeds the detection threshold. The signal strength is the population conditional dependence measure under g:

Remark 41 (Comparison with the raw-statistic analysis) . The mean expansion (272) has a strikingly simpler form than the corresponding expansion for the raw statistic in the earlier lit-erature, where two separate dimension-dependent geometric-bias terms appear at rates n1/2−2/D 

and n1/2−2/d Z corresponding to the two neighborhood scales. In the debiased version, both these geometric biases are killed by the polynomial reproduction, and only the smaller O(( kn/n )(p+1) /D )

residual remains. Under the bias killing condition (136) , √n B (p) 

> n

→ 0, and the only √n-scale contribution to the centering is the population signal √n T (Pn).

## J.4 Concluding remarks 

Remark 42 (Curse-of-dimensionality is encoded in the bandwidth, not the threshold) . A central observation of the debiased framework is that the detection threshold r∗ 

> n

= n−1/4 is dimension-free : it does not deteriorate with D = dZ + dX . The dimensional dependence is instead absorbed into the bandwidth constraint α < 1 − D/ (2( p + 1)) (Lemma 14), which forces larger polynomial orders p in higher dimensions. Once a valid (kn, p ) pair is chosen, however, the test achieves the parametric n−1/4 detection threshold against any fixed-direction local alternative. This contrasts sharply with the raw-statistic analysis, where the dimensional dependence enters the detection threshold itself via the two geometric bias terms at rates n1/2−2/D and n1/2−2/d Z ,producing a phase transition at D = 8 between an n−1/4 regime and an n2/D −1/2 regime. The local-polynomial debiasing eliminates this phase transition by removing the geometric bias terms; the curse of dimensionality persists only in the form of the bandwidth-polynomial-order constraint. 

Remark 43 (Comparison with the unconditional rate) . The n−1/4 detection threshold for the mixture parameter rn in Theorem 16 matches the corresponding threshold for two sample test based on geometric graph (in lower dimension) against parametric mixture alternatives [Bhattacharya, 2019]. Whether this rate is minimax optimal over a suitable smoothness class of conditional dependence structures, analogous to the Sobolev-ball minimax theory developed in Albert et al. [2022] for unconditional independence, is an open question we do not pursue here. 

Remark 44. The condition Var n(√n ∆(p) 

> n

)/ Var 0(√n ∆(p) 

> n

) → 1 is a mild contiguity-type require-ment. Under contiguous mixtures with rn → 0, the variance of √n ∆(p) 

> n

under Pn converges to its variance under P0 because the variance functional is continuous in the joint law. Formal verifica-tion follows from the explicit overlap-variance formula (156) expressed as a continuous functional of the joint distribution; for rn → 0, the perturbation contributes O(rn) to the variance, which is negligible.

# K Efficient Conditional Inpendence Test Implementation 

Here we provide detailed algorithms and theoretical guarantees for the implementation of condi-tional independence tests constructed under different data type scenarios. 

## K.1 Implementation for Discrete Neighborhoods 

As stated in the main text, when handling cases where all variables concerning neighborhood construction are discrete, we would adopt a gaussian bootstrap approach. The details are provided in the Algorithm 1. To ensure the empirical p-value gained in above algorithm is valid, we need to establish the consistency of bootstrap process in the discrete regime. The details and proof are provided in Proposition 17. 

Proposition 17 (GMB Consistency in the Discrete Regime) . Suppose the conditioning sets are fully discrete, such that exact matching is used, and the observations W = {W1, . . . , W n} are independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.). Under the null hypothesis H0 : Y ⊥⊥ X | Z, the Gaussian Multiplier Bootstrap (GMB) statistic T ∗ 

> n

consistently estimates the null distribution of 

Tn. That is, 

sup 

> t∈R

|P∗(T ∗ 

> n

≤ t | W) − P(Tn ≤ t)| p

−→ 0.

Proof. We verify the regularity conditions for the multiplier bootstrap consistency of degenerate 

U -statistics under i.i.d. sampling, as established by Dehling and Mikosch [1994] (see also Arcones and Gin´ e [1992]). First, the empirical test statistic Tn is a standard second-order U -statistic: 

Tn =

(n

2

)−1 ∑

> 1≤i<j ≤n

h(Wi, W j ),

where the symmetrized difference kernel h is symmetric by construction. Second, we verify the square-integrability of the kernel. In our discrete regime, h consists of the indicator function K(Yi, Y j ) = 1(Yi = Yj ) and the inverse neighborhood counts 1 /f i and 1 /c i.92 Since every data point is at least a neighbor to itself, the counts satisfy fi ≥ 1 and ci ≥ 1. Thus, the kernel is uniformly bounded ( ∥h∥∞ < ∞), which trivially implies finite second moments: 

E[h(Wi, W j )2] < ∞.

Notice that this condition completely avoids the need for the kernel to be positive semi-definite, which is often required in dependent data settings but unnecessary here. Third, we establish the degeneracy of h under H0. As shown in Section D, exact matching ensures zero smoothing bias. Therefore, the first-order conditional expectation vanishes almost surely: 

g(Wi) = E[h(Wi, W j ) | Wi] = 0 a.s. This confirms that Tn is a strictly degenerate U -statistic. Finally, the GMB statistic is defined as: 

T ∗ 

> n

=

> (

n

2 

> )−1∑
> 1≤i<j ≤n

ξiξj h(Wi, W j ),

where {ξi}ni=1 

> i.i.d.

∼ N (0 , 1) are independent of the data. Since h is symmetric, square-integrable, and perfectly degenerate under H0, all required conditions for the Gaussian multiplier boot-strap are satisfied [Dehling and Mikosch, 1994]. Consequently, the conditional distribution of T ∗

> n

asymptotically correctly estimates the unconditional distribution of Tn.

## K.2 Implementation for Continuous or Mixed Neighborhoods 

When the neighborhood construction concerns continuous variables, we implement two algo-rithms: one is to use the locally debiased test statistic and other is to introduce a conditional permutation in the unbiased test statistic to estimate the null hypothesis. The detailed algorithm is provided below in Algorithm 2. 

## K.3 Details of the Data Generating Mechanisms 

To rigorously evaluate the proposed conditional independence test, we generate synthetic data (X, Y, Z ) of sample size n across four distinct data regimes: purely continuous (CCC), mixed with discrete Z (CCD), mixed with discrete X (DCC), and purely discrete (DDD). For all continuous variables, the additive noise is sampled as ϵ ∼ N (0 , σ 2), where the scale σ

is uniformly drawn from [0 .1, 0.3]. The scaling coefficients are independently sampled uniformly as axz , a yz ∼ U (0 .5, 1.5) and ayx ∼ U (0 .3, 0.8). For continuous nonlinear mechanisms, a function 

f (·) is chosen uniformly at random from the set {sin( x), cos( x), tanh( x), x 2/(1 + x2)}. We use 

D(V, k ) to denote the operation that discretizes a continuous variable V into k equal-frequency bins (quantile binning). We define the conditional independence null hypothesis H0 : X ⊥ Y | Z and the alternative hypothesis H1 : X̸ ⊥ Y | Z. The data generation process for each regime is described below. 

K.3.1 Regime 1: CCC (Purely Continuous) 

In this regime, all variables X, Y , and Z are continuous. 

• Linear : Z ∼ U (−2, 2), and X = axz Z + ϵX . Under H0, Y = ayz Z + ϵY . Under H1,

Y = ayz Z + ayx X + ϵY .

• Nonlinear : Z ∼ U (−2, 2), and X = f (axz Z) + ϵX . Under H0, Y = f (ayz Z) + ϵY . Under 

H1, Y = f (ayz Z) + ayx f (X) + ϵY .93 K.3.2 Regime 2: CCD (Continuous X, Y , Discrete Z)

Here, Z is a discrete categorical variable with k ∈ { 2, 3, 4} categories. 

• Linear : Z ∼ Uniform {0, . . . , k − 1}. Let cx,Z and cy,Z be category-specific effects linearly spaced in [ −2, 2] and [2 , −2], respectively. X = axz cx,Z + ϵX . Under H0, Y = ayz cy,Z + ϵY .Under H1, Y = ayz cy,Z + ayx X + ϵY .

• Nonlinear : X ∼ N (0 , 1). The variable Z is drawn from a Bernoulli distribution with probability pz = 1 /(1 + exp( −X)). Under H0, Y = sin( Zπ ) + ϵY . Under H1, Y =sin( Zπ ) + 0 .8X2 + ϵY .

K.3.3 Regime 3: DCC (Discrete X, Continuous Y, Z )

The continuous variables influence or are influenced by a discrete X with k ∈ { 3, 4, 5} categories. 

• Linear : Z ∼ U (−2, 2). A latent variable X∗ = axz Z + ϵX is generated and discretized to 

X = D(X∗, k ). Under H0, Y = ayz Z + ϵY . Under H1, Y = ayz Z + ayx X + ϵY .

• Nonlinear : Z ∼ U (−2, 2). A latent variable X∗ = f (axz Z) + ϵX is generated and dis-cretized to X = D(X∗, k ). Under H0, Y = f (ayz Z) + ϵY . Under H1, Y = f (ayz Z) + 

ayx (X mod 2) + ϵY .

K.3.4 Regime 4: DDD (Purely Discrete) 

All variables are generated as discrete integers modulo their respective category bounds nx, n y, n z ∈{3, 4, 5}. Discrete noise terms ϵd,X ∈ { 0, 1, 2} and ϵd,Y ∈ { 0, 1} are used. 

• Linear : Z ∼ Uniform {0, . . . , n z − 1}. X = ( Z + ϵd,X ) mod nx. Under H0, Y = ( Z +

ϵd,Y ) mod ny. Under H1, Y = ( Z + X + ϵd,Y ) mod ny.

• Nonlinear : Z ∼ Uniform {0, . . . , n z − 1}. X = ( Z2 + ϵd,X ) mod nx. Under H0, Y =(Z2 + ϵd,Y ) mod ny. Under H1, Y = ( Z · X + ϵd,Y ) mod ny.

K.3.5 Data Generating Processes for Heterogeneous Mixed Confounding 

To evaluate the robustness of conditional independence tests against non-ordinal categorical con-founders, we define a parameterized class of Data Generating Processes (DGPs). Let Z = ( Zc, Z d)be the mixed confounding set, where Zc ∈ R is a continuous covariate and Zd ∈ { 0, 1, . . . , K − 1}

is a discrete categorical covariate. We generate Zc ∼ N (0 , 1) and draw Zd uniformly from the discrete categories (we set K = 8 in our experiments). To construct an adversarial setting for unified metric-based methods, we introduce a non-ordinal “sawtooth” shift function S(Zd) controlled by a heterogeneity amplitude parameter δ ≥ 0: 

S(Zd) = δ · (Zd mod 2) This function induces a jump in the conditional means of X and Y depending on whether the categorical index is even or odd. Global smooth kernels will inadvertently smooth across these jumps, creating a shared residual error that manifests as spurious correlation. The generation of X and Y follows the general structural equations: 

X = S(Zd) + Zc + ϵX

Y = S(Zd) + f (Zc) + βX · IH1 + ϵY

where ϵX , ϵ Y ∼ N (0 , σ 2) are independent Gaussian noise terms, and IH1 is the indicator function for the alternative hypothesis (i.e., IH1 = 0 under the null hypothesis X ⊥⊥ Y | Z, and 1 otherwise). The parameter β controls the signal strength under H1.94 To isolate the topological distortion of the neighborhood manifold, we maintain a linear rela-tionship between X and Zc, ensuring the geometric flatness of the localized sub-manifolds. The nonlinearity is exclusively injected into the generation of Y via the function f (Zc): 

• Linear Mechanism: We set f (Zc) = Zc.

• Nonlinear Mechanism: We set f (Zc) = Zc + αZ 2 

> c

, introducing a smooth quadratic curvature. In our empirical evaluations, we set α = 0 .5 and β = 1 .2. By varying δ ∈ [0 , 0.30], we control the severity of the discrete heterogeneity. Our exact stratification approach perfectly decouples the S(Zd) term, effectively rendering the Type-I error of MixCIT invariant to δ, whereas methods lacking exact discrete matching suffer from severe Type-I error inflation proportional to O(δ2).

Set ( D, C) ← (discrete, continuous) columns of ( Z, X ) if side = fine, else of Z 

> 19:

Partition rows into strata {Gℓ} by exact match on D 

> 20:

for each stratum Gℓ do  

> 21:

Ni ← kn-NN of i in Gℓ w.r.t. standardized C // Ni = Gℓ \ { i} if C = ∅ 

> 22:

ρi ← Euclidean distance to the kn-th neighbor in Ni // data-driven bandwidth  

> 23:

Uij ← standardized continuous offsets ( Cj − C i) for j ∈ N i 

> 24:

end for  

> 25:

return (Ni, U ij , ρ i)ni=1  

> 26:

end procedure
